<div class="mw-body-content mw-content-ltr" dir="ltr" id="mw-content-text" lang="en"><div class="mw-parser-output"><div class="shortdescription nomobile noexcerpt noprint searchaux" style="display:none">1526–1857 empire in South Asia</div>
<style data-mw-deduplicate="TemplateStyles:r1033289096">.mw-parser-output .hatnote{font-style:italic}.mw-parser-output div.hatnote{padding-left:1.6em;margin-bottom:0.5em}.mw-parser-output .hatnote i{font-style:normal}.mw-parser-output .hatnote+link+.hatnote{margin-top:-0.5em}</style><div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Not to be confused with the <a href="/wiki/Mongol_Empire" title="Mongol Empire">Mongol Empire</a> or <a href="/wiki/Moghulistan" title="Moghulistan">Moghulistan</a>.</div>
<link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1033289096" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">"Mughals" redirects here. For the ethnic groups, see <a href="/wiki/Mughal_people" title="Mughal people">Mughal people</a>.</div>

<p>The <b>Mughal Empire</b> was an <a href="/wiki/Islam" title="Islam">Islamic</a> <a href="/wiki/Early_modern_period" title="Early modern period">early-modern</a> empire that controlled much of <a href="/wiki/South_Asia" title="South Asia">South Asia</a> between the 16th and 19th centuries.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Richards1995-2_12-0"><a href="#cite_note-Richards1995-2-12">[11]</a></sup> For some two hundred years, the empire stretched from the outer fringes of the <a href="/wiki/Indus_River" title="Indus River">Indus river basin</a> in the west, northern <a href="/wiki/Afghanistan" title="Afghanistan">Afghanistan</a> in the northwest, and <a href="/wiki/Kashmir" title="Kashmir">Kashmir</a> in the north, to the <a href="/wiki/Highland" title="Highland">highlands</a> of present-day <a href="/wiki/Assam" title="Assam">Assam</a> and <a href="/wiki/Bangladesh" title="Bangladesh">Bangladesh</a> in the east, and the uplands of the <a href="/wiki/Deccan_Plateau" title="Deccan Plateau">Deccan Plateau</a> in <a href="/wiki/South_India" title="South India">South India</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Stein2010-12_13-0"><a href="#cite_note-Stein2010-12-13">[12]</a></sup>
</p><p>The Mughal Empire is conventionally said to have been founded in 1526 by <a href="/wiki/Babur" title="Babur">Babur</a>, a warrior <a href="/wiki/Tribal_chief" title="Tribal chief">chieftain</a> from what is today <a href="/wiki/Uzbekistan" title="Uzbekistan">Uzbekistan</a>, who employed aid from the neighboring <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Safavid_empire" title="Safavid empire">Safavid</a> and <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Ottoman_empire" title="Ottoman empire">Ottoman</a> empires,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Gilbert2017_14-0"><a href="#cite_note-Gilbert2017-14">[13]</a></sup> to defeat the <a href="/wiki/Delhi_Sultanate" title="Delhi Sultanate">Sultan of Delhi</a>, <a href="/wiki/Ibrahim_Lodhi" title="Ibrahim Lodhi">Ibrahim Lodhi</a>, in the <a href="/wiki/First_Battle_of_Panipat" title="First Battle of Panipat">First Battle of Panipat</a>, and to sweep down the plains of <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Upper_India" title="Upper India">Upper India</a>. The Mughal imperial structure, however, is sometimes dated to 1600, to the rule of Babur's grandson, <a href="/wiki/Akbar" title="Akbar">Akbar</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Stein2010_15-0"><a href="#cite_note-Stein2010-15">[14]</a></sup> This imperial structure lasted until 1720, until shortly after the death of the last major emperor, <a href="/wiki/Aurangzeb" title="Aurangzeb">Aurangzeb</a>,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Stein2010-1_16-0"><a href="#cite_note-Stein2010-1-16">[15]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Richards1995-3_17-0"><a href="#cite_note-Richards1995-3-17">[16]</a></sup> during whose reign the empire also achieved its maximum geographical extent. The empire's loss of control over the Indian subcontinent was catalyzed by their loss to the <a href="/wiki/Maratha_Empire" title="Maratha Empire">Maratha Empire</a> in the <a href="/wiki/Mughal%E2%80%93Maratha_Wars" title="Mughal–Maratha Wars">Mughal–Maratha Wars</a>. Reduced subsequently to the region in and around <a href="/wiki/Old_Delhi" title="Old Delhi">Old Delhi</a> by 1760, the empire was formally dissolved by the <a href="/wiki/British_Raj" title="British Raj">British Raj</a> after the <a href="/wiki/Indian_Rebellion_of_1857" title="Indian Rebellion of 1857">Indian Rebellion of 1857</a>. 
</p><p>Although the Mughal Empire was created and sustained by military warfare,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Stein2010-2_18-0"><a href="#cite_note-Stein2010-2-18">[17]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Robb2011_19-0"><a href="#cite_note-Robb2011-19">[18]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Gilbert2017-2_20-0"><a href="#cite_note-Gilbert2017-2-20">[19]</a></sup> it did not vigorously suppress the cultures and peoples it came to rule; rather it equalized and placated them through new administrative practices,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-21"><a href="#cite_note-21">[20]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-22"><a href="#cite_note-22">[21]</a></sup> and diverse ruling elites, leading to more efficient, centralised, and standardized rule.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-AsherTalbot2006-2_23-0"><a href="#cite_note-AsherTalbot2006-2-23">[22]</a></sup> The base of the empire's collective wealth was agricultural taxes, instituted by the third Mughal emperor, Akbar.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Stein2010-3_24-0"><a href="#cite_note-Stein2010-3-24">[23]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-AsherTalbot2006-1_25-0"><a href="#cite_note-AsherTalbot2006-1-25">[24]</a></sup> These taxes, which amounted to well over half the output of a peasant cultivator,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Stein2010-4_26-0"><a href="#cite_note-Stein2010-4-26">[25]</a></sup> were paid in the well-regulated silver currency,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-AsherTalbot2006-2_23-1"><a href="#cite_note-AsherTalbot2006-2-23">[22]</a></sup> and caused peasants and artisans to enter larger markets.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-AsherTalbot2006-3_27-0"><a href="#cite_note-AsherTalbot2006-3-27">[26]</a></sup>
</p><p>The relative peace maintained by the empire during much of the 17th century was a factor in India's economic expansion.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-AsherTalbot2006-4_28-0"><a href="#cite_note-AsherTalbot2006-4-28">[27]</a></sup> Burgeoning European presence in the Indian Ocean, and its increasing demand for Indian raw and finished products, created still greater wealth in the Mughal courts.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-AsherTalbot2006-5_29-0"><a href="#cite_note-AsherTalbot2006-5-29">[28]</a></sup> There was more conspicuous consumption among the Mughal elite,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-AsherTalbot2006-6_30-0"><a href="#cite_note-AsherTalbot2006-6-30">[29]</a></sup> resulting in greater patronage of <a href="/wiki/Mughal_painting" title="Mughal painting">painting</a>, literary forms, textiles, and <a href="/wiki/Mughal_architecture" title="Mughal architecture">architecture</a>, especially during the reign of <a href="/wiki/Shah_Jahan" title="Shah Jahan">Shah Jahan</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-AsherTalbot2006-7_31-0"><a href="#cite_note-AsherTalbot2006-7-31">[30]</a></sup> Among the Mughal <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/UNESCO_World_Heritage_Sites" title="UNESCO World Heritage Sites">UNESCO World Heritage Sites</a> in South Asia are: <a href="/wiki/Agra_Fort" title="Agra Fort">Agra Fort</a>, <a href="/wiki/Fatehpur_Sikri" title="Fatehpur Sikri">Fatehpur Sikri</a>, <a href="/wiki/Red_Fort" title="Red Fort">Red Fort</a>, <a href="/wiki/Humayun%27s_Tomb" title="Humayun's Tomb">Humayun's Tomb</a>, <a href="/wiki/Lahore_Fort" title="Lahore Fort">Lahore Fort</a>, <a href="/wiki/Shalimar_Gardens,_Lahore" title="Shalimar Gardens, Lahore">Shalimar Gardens</a> and the <a href="/wiki/Taj_Mahal" title="Taj Mahal">Taj Mahal</a>.
</p>

<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Name">Name</span></h2>
<p>Contemporaries referred to the empire founded by <a href="/wiki/Babur" title="Babur">Babur</a> as the <a href="/wiki/Timurid_Empire" title="Timurid Empire">Timurid Empire</a>,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-MSA_32-0"><a href="#cite_note-MSA-32">[31]</a></sup> which reflected the heritage of his dynasty, and this was the term preferred by the Mughals themselves.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Avari_33-0"><a href="#cite_note-Avari-33">[32]</a></sup>
</p><p>The Mughal designation for their own dynasty was <b>Gurkani</b> (<a href="/wiki/Persian_language" title="Persian language">Persian</a>: <span dir="rtl" lang="fa"><span class="Nastaliq" dir="rtl" style="font-family: 'Jameel Noori Nastaleeq', 'Urdu Typesetting', 'Noto Nastaliq Urdu', 'Noto Nastaliq Urdu Draft', 'Hussaini Nastaleeq', 'AlQalam Taj Nastaleeq', IranNastaliq, 'Awami Nastaliq', 'Awami Nastaliq Beta3', 'Awami Nastaliq Beta2', 'Awami Nastaliq Beta1', 'Nafees Nastaleeq', 'Nafees Nastaleeq v1.01', 'Pak Nastaleeq', 'PDMS_Jauhar', 'Alvi Lahori Nastaleeq'; font-size: 110%; font-style: normal;" title="Nastaliq">گورکانیان</span></span>, <small><a href="/wiki/Romanization_of_Persian" title="Romanization of Persian">romanized</a>: </small><span title="Persian-language romanization"><i lang="fa-Latn">Gūrkāniyān</i></span>, <small><a href="/wiki/Literal_translation" title="Literal translation">lit.</a> </small>'sons-in-law').<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Thackston2_34-0"><a href="#cite_note-Thackston2-34">[33]</a></sup> The use of "Mughal" and "Moghul" derived from the <a href="/wiki/Arabic" title="Arabic">Arabic</a> and <a href="/wiki/Persian_language" title="Persian language">Persian</a> corruption of "<a href="/wiki/Mongols" title="Mongols">Mongol</a>", and it emphasised the Mongol origins of the Timurid dynasty.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Hodgson_35-0"><a href="#cite_note-Hodgson-35">[34]</a></sup> The term gained currency during the 19th century, but remains disputed by <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Indologists" title="Indologists">Indologists</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-RA_36-0"><a href="#cite_note-RA-36">[35]</a></sup> Similar transliterations had been used to refer to the empire, including "Mogul" and "Moghul".<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-persianatemogul_37-0"><a href="#cite_note-persianatemogul-37">[36]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTERutherford2010_38-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTERutherford2010-38">[37]</a></sup> Nevertheless, Babur's ancestors were sharply distinguished from the classical Mongols insofar as they were oriented towards <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Persian_culture" title="Persian culture">Persian</a> rather than <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Turco-Mongol" title="Turco-Mongol">Turco-Mongol</a> culture. The Mughals themselves claimed ultimate descent from Mongol Empire founder <a href="/wiki/Genghis_Khan" title="Genghis Khan">Genghis Khan</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Canfield_39-0"><a href="#cite_note-Canfield-39">[38]</a></sup>
</p><p>Another name for the empire was <a href="/wiki/Hindustan" title="Hindustan">Hindustan</a>, which was documented in the <a href="/wiki/Ain-i-Akbari" title="Ain-i-Akbari">Ain-i-Akbari</a>, and which has been described as the closest to an official name for the empire.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Vanina_40-0"><a href="#cite_note-Vanina-40">[39]</a></sup> In the west, the term "<a href="/wiki/Mughal_emperors" title="Mughal emperors">Mughal</a>" was used for the emperor, and by extension, the empire as a whole.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Fontana_41-0"><a href="#cite_note-Fontana-41">[40]</a></sup>
</p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="History">History</span></h2>

<h3><span id="Babur_and_Humayun_.281526.E2.80.931556.29"></span><span class="mw-headline" id="Babur_and_Humayun_(1526–1556)">Babur and Humayun (1526–1556)</span></h3>
<link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1033289096" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Main articles: <a href="/wiki/Babur" title="Babur">Babur</a> and <a href="/wiki/Humayun" title="Humayun">Humayun</a></div>

</div>
<div style="visibility:hidden"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:South_Asia_non_political,_with_rivers.jpg"><img alt="" data-file-height="2151" data-file-width="2324" decoding="async" height="268" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/5/52/South_Asia_non_political%2C_with_rivers.jpg/290px-South_Asia_non_political%2C_with_rivers.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/5/52/South_Asia_non_political%2C_with_rivers.jpg/435px-South_Asia_non_political%2C_with_rivers.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/5/52/South_Asia_non_political%2C_with_rivers.jpg/580px-South_Asia_non_political%2C_with_rivers.jpg 2x" width="290"/></a></div>
</div>
<div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:South_Asia_non_political,_with_rivers.jpg" title="File:South Asia non political, with rivers.jpg"> </a></div>Main South Asian polities circa 1525 CE, on the eve of the establishment of the Mughal Empire by <a href="/wiki/Babur" title="Babur">Babur</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-42"><a href="#cite_note-42">[41]</a></sup></div>
</div></div>
<p>The Mughal Empire was founded by Babur (reigned 1526–1530), a Central Asian ruler who was descended from the <a href="/wiki/Turco-Mongol_tradition" title="Turco-Mongol tradition">Turco-Mongol</a> conqueror <a href="/wiki/Timur" title="Timur">Timur</a> (the founder of the <a href="/wiki/Timurid_Empire" title="Timurid Empire">Timurid Empire</a>) on his father's side, and from <a href="/wiki/Genghis_Khan" title="Genghis Khan">Genghis Khan</a> on his mother's side.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Berndl_43-0"><a href="#cite_note-Berndl-43">[42]</a></sup> Ousted from his ancestral domains in Central Asia, Babur turned to India to satisfy his ambitions.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-44"><a href="#cite_note-44">[43]</a></sup>
He established himself in <a href="/wiki/Kabul" title="Kabul">Kabul</a> and then pushed steadily southward into India from <a href="/wiki/Afghanistan" title="Afghanistan">Afghanistan</a> through the <a href="/wiki/Khyber_Pass" title="Khyber Pass">Khyber Pass</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Berndl_43-1"><a href="#cite_note-Berndl-43">[42]</a></sup> Babur's forces defeated Ibrahim Lodhi in the <a href="/wiki/First_Battle_of_Panipat" title="First Battle of Panipat">First Battle of Panipat</a>. Before the battle, Babur sought divine favour by abjuring liquor, breaking the wine vessels and pouring the wine down a well.
However, by this time <a href="/wiki/Delhi_Sultanate" title="Delhi Sultanate">Lodhi's empire</a> was already crumbling, and it was actually the <a href="/wiki/Rajput" title="Rajput">Rajput Confederacy</a> which was the strongest power of Northern India under the capable rule of <a href="/wiki/Rana_Sanga" title="Rana Sanga">Rana Sanga</a> of <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Udaipur_State" title="Udaipur State">Mewar</a>. He defeated Babar in the <a href="/wiki/Battle_of_Bayana" title="Battle of Bayana">Battle of Bayana</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-JP_45-0"><a href="#cite_note-JP-45">[44]</a></sup> In a decisive battle fought near Agra, the Timurid forces of Babur defeated the Rajput army of Sanga. This battle was one of the most decisive and historic battles in Indian history, as it sealed the fate of Northern India for the next two centuries.
</p><p>After the battle, the centre of Mughal power became Agra instead of Kabul. The preoccupation with wars and military campaigns, however, did not allow the new emperor to consolidate the gains he had made in India.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-46"><a href="#cite_note-46">[45]</a></sup> The instability of the empire became evident under his son, <a href="/wiki/Humayun" title="Humayun">Humayun</a> (reigned 1530–1556), who was forced into exile in Persia by rebels. The <a href="/wiki/Sur_Empire" title="Sur Empire">Sur Empire</a> (1540–1555), founded by <a href="/wiki/Sher_Shah_Suri" title="Sher Shah Suri">Sher Shah Suri</a> (reigned 1540–1545), briefly interrupted Mughal rule.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Berndl_43-2"><a href="#cite_note-Berndl-43">[42]</a></sup> Humayun's exile in Persia established diplomatic ties between the <a href="/wiki/Safavid_dynasty" title="Safavid dynasty">Safavid</a> and Mughal Courts, and led to increasing Persian cultural influence in the later restored Mughal Empire.<sup class="noprint Inline-Template Template-Fact" style="white-space:nowrap;">[<i><a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Citation_needed" title="Wikipedia:Citation needed"><span title="This claim needs references to reliable sources. (July 2019)">citation needed</span></a></i>]</sup> Humayun's triumphant return from Persia in 1555 restored Mughal rule in some parts of India, but he died in an accident the next year.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Berndl_43-3"><a href="#cite_note-Berndl-43">[42]</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span id="Akbar_to_Aurangzeb_.281556.E2.80.931707.29"></span><span class="mw-headline" id="Akbar_to_Aurangzeb_(1556–1707)">Akbar to Aurangzeb (1556–1707)</span></h3>
<link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1033289096" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Main articles: <a href="/wiki/Akbar" title="Akbar">Akbar</a>, <a href="/wiki/Jahangir" title="Jahangir">Jahangir</a>, <a href="/wiki/Shah_Jahan" title="Shah Jahan">Shah Jahan</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Aurangzeb" title="Aurangzeb">Aurangzeb</a></div>
<div class="thumb tleft"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:172px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Jesuits_at_Akbar%27s_court.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="4123" data-file-width="2275" decoding="async" height="308" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/d/d1/Jesuits_at_Akbar%27s_court.jpg/170px-Jesuits_at_Akbar%27s_court.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/d/d1/Jesuits_at_Akbar%27s_court.jpg/255px-Jesuits_at_Akbar%27s_court.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/d/d1/Jesuits_at_Akbar%27s_court.jpg/340px-Jesuits_at_Akbar%27s_court.jpg 2x" width="170"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Jesuits_at_Akbar%27s_court.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div><a href="/wiki/Akbar" title="Akbar">Akbar</a> holds a religious assembly of different faiths in the <a href="/wiki/Ibadat_Khana" title="Ibadat Khana">Ibadat Khana</a> in Fatehpur Sikri.</div></div></div>
<p><a href="/wiki/Akbar" title="Akbar">Akbar</a> (reigned 1556–1605) was born Jalal-ud-din Muhammad<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-47"><a href="#cite_note-47">[46]</a></sup> in the Rajput <a href="/wiki/Umarkot_Fort" title="Umarkot Fort">Umarkot Fort</a>,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-48"><a href="#cite_note-48">[47]</a></sup> to Humayun and his wife <a href="/wiki/Hamida_Banu_Begum" title="Hamida Banu Begum">Hamida Banu Begum</a>, a <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Persian_people" title="Persian people">Persian</a> princess.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-49"><a href="#cite_note-49">[48]</a></sup> Akbar succeeded to the throne under a regent, <a href="/wiki/Bairam_Khan" title="Bairam Khan">Bairam Khan</a>, who helped consolidate the Mughal Empire in India. Through warfare and diplomacy, Akbar was able to extend the empire in all directions and controlled almost the entire Indian subcontinent north of the <a href="/wiki/Godavari_River" title="Godavari River">Godavari River</a>.<sup class="noprint Inline-Template Template-Fact" style="white-space:nowrap;">[<i><a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Citation_needed" title="Wikipedia:Citation needed"><span title="This claim needs references to reliable sources. (July 2019)">citation needed</span></a></i>]</sup> He created a new ruling elite loyal to him, implemented a modern administration, and encouraged cultural developments. He increased trade with European trading companies.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Berndl_43-4"><a href="#cite_note-Berndl-43">[42]</a></sup> India developed a strong and stable economy, leading to commercial expansion and economic development.<sup class="noprint Inline-Template Template-Fact" style="white-space:nowrap;">[<i><a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Citation_needed" title="Wikipedia:Citation needed"><span title="This claim needs references to reliable sources. (July 2019)">citation needed</span></a></i>]</sup> Akbar allowed freedom of religion at his court, and attempted to resolve socio-political and cultural differences in his empire by establishing a new religion, <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Din-i-Ilahi" title="Din-i-Ilahi">Din-i-Ilahi</a>, with strong characteristics of a ruler cult.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Berndl_43-5"><a href="#cite_note-Berndl-43">[42]</a></sup> He left his son an internally stable state, which was in the midst of its golden age, but before long signs of political weakness would emerge.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Berndl_43-6"><a href="#cite_note-Berndl-43">[42]</a></sup>
</p><p><a href="/wiki/Jahangir" title="Jahangir">Jahangir</a> (born Salim,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Mohammada_50-0"><a href="#cite_note-Mohammada-50">[49]</a></sup> reigned 1605–1627) was born to Akbar and his wife <a href="/wiki/Mariam-uz-Zamani" title="Mariam-uz-Zamani">Mariam-uz-Zamani</a>, an Indian <a href="/wiki/Rajput" title="Rajput">Rajput</a> princess.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-51"><a href="#cite_note-51">[50]</a></sup> Salim was named after the Indian Sufi saint, <a href="/wiki/Salim_Chishti" title="Salim Chishti">Salim Chishti</a> and was raised by the daughter of Chishti.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-52"><a href="#cite_note-52">[51]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-53"><a href="#cite_note-53">[52]</a></sup> He "was addicted to opium, neglected the affairs of the state, and came under the influence of rival court cliques".<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Berndl_43-7"><a href="#cite_note-Berndl-43">[42]</a></sup> Jahangir distinguished himself from Akbar by making substantial efforts to gain the support of the Islamic religious establishment. One way he did this was by bestowing many more <i>madad-i-ma'ash</i> (tax-free personal land revenue grants given to religiously learned or spiritually worthy individuals) than Akbar had.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-54"><a href="#cite_note-54">[53]</a></sup> In contrast to Akbar, Jahangir came into conflict with non-Muslim religious leaders, notably the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Sikh" title="Sikh">Sikh</a> guru <a href="/wiki/Guru_Arjan" title="Guru Arjan">Arjan</a>, whose execution was the first of many conflicts between the Mughal empire and the Sikh community.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Robb_97-98_55-0"><a href="#cite_note-Robb_97-98-55">[54]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-AsherTalbot2006-8_56-0"><a href="#cite_note-AsherTalbot2006-8-56">[55]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-BBC_Sikhs_57-0"><a href="#cite_note-BBC_Sikhs-57">[56]</a></sup>
</p>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Khalili_Collection_Islamic_Art_mss_0874.3.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="2500" data-file-width="3750" decoding="async" height="147" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/a/a3/Khalili_Collection_Islamic_Art_mss_0874.3.jpg/220px-Khalili_Collection_Islamic_Art_mss_0874.3.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/a/a3/Khalili_Collection_Islamic_Art_mss_0874.3.jpg/330px-Khalili_Collection_Islamic_Art_mss_0874.3.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/a/a3/Khalili_Collection_Islamic_Art_mss_0874.3.jpg/440px-Khalili_Collection_Islamic_Art_mss_0874.3.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Khalili_Collection_Islamic_Art_mss_0874.3.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Group portrait of Mughal rulers, from <a href="/wiki/Babur" title="Babur">Babur</a> to <a href="/wiki/Aurangzeb" title="Aurangzeb">Aurangzeb</a>, with the Mughal ancestor <a href="/wiki/Timur" title="Timur">Timur</a> seated in the middle. On the left: <a href="/wiki/Shah_Jahan" title="Shah Jahan">Shah Jahan</a>, <a href="/wiki/Akbar" title="Akbar">Akbar</a> and Babur, with Abu Sa'id of Samarkand and Timur's son, <a href="/wiki/Miran_Shah" title="Miran Shah">Miran Shah</a>. On the right: Aurangzeb, <a href="/wiki/Jahangir" title="Jahangir">Jahangir</a> and <a href="/wiki/Humayun" title="Humayun">Humayun</a>, and two of Timur's other offspring <a href="/wiki/Umar_Shaikh_Mirza_I" title="Umar Shaikh Mirza I">Umar Shaykh</a> and <a href="/wiki/Muhammad_Sultan_Mirza" title="Muhammad Sultan Mirza">Muhammad Sultan</a>. Created <abbr title="circa">c.</abbr><span style="white-space:nowrap;"> 1707–12</span></div></div></div>
<p><a href="/wiki/Shah_Jahan" title="Shah Jahan">Shah Jahan</a> (reigned 1628–1658) was born to Jahangir and his wife <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Jagat_Gosaini" title="Jagat Gosaini">Jagat Gosaini</a>, a Rajput princess.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Mohammada_50-1"><a href="#cite_note-Mohammada-50">[49]</a></sup> His reign ushered in the golden age of <a href="/wiki/Mughal_architecture" title="Mughal architecture">Mughal architecture</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-58"><a href="#cite_note-58">[57]</a></sup> During the reign of Shah Jahan, the splendour of the Mughal court reached its peak, as exemplified by the <a href="/wiki/Taj_Mahal" title="Taj Mahal">Taj Mahal</a>.The cost of maintaining the court, however, began to exceed the revenue coming in.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Berndl_43-8"><a href="#cite_note-Berndl-43">[42]</a></sup> His reign was called as "The Golden Age of Mughal Architecture". Shah Jahan extended the Mughal empire to the Deccan by ending the Nizam Shahi dynasty, and forced the Adil Shahis and Qutb Shahis to pay tribute.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-59"><a href="#cite_note-59">[58]</a></sup>
</p><p>Shah Jahan's eldest son, the liberal <a href="/wiki/Dara_Shikoh" title="Dara Shikoh">Dara Shikoh</a>, became regent in 1658, as a result of his father's illness.<sup class="noprint Inline-Template Template-Fact" style="white-space:nowrap;">[<i><a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Citation_needed" title="Wikipedia:Citation needed"><span title="This claim needs references to reliable sources. (July 2019)">citation needed</span></a></i>]</sup> Dara championed a syncretistic Hindu-Muslim culture, emulating his great-grandfather Akbar.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-60"><a href="#cite_note-60">[59]</a></sup> With the support of the Islamic orthodoxy, however, a younger son of Shah Jahan, <a href="/wiki/Aurangzeb" title="Aurangzeb">Aurangzeb</a> (<span style="white-space:nowrap;"><abbr title="reigned">r.</abbr> 1658–1707</span>), seized the throne. Aurangzeb defeated Dara in 1659 and had him executed.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Berndl_43-9"><a href="#cite_note-Berndl-43">[42]</a></sup> Although Shah Jahan fully recovered from his illness, Aurangzeb kept Shah Jahan imprisoned until his death in 1666.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Truschke_61-0"><a href="#cite_note-Truschke-61">[60]</a></sup><sup class="reference nowrap"><span title="Page / location: 68">: 68 </span></sup> Aurangzeb oversaw an increase in the Islamicization of the Mughal state. He encouraged conversion to Islam, reinstated the <i><a href="/wiki/Jizya" title="Jizya">jizya</a></i> on non-Muslims, and compiled the <i><a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Fatawa_al-Alamgir" title="Fatawa al-Alamgir">Fatawa al-Alamgir</a></i>, a collection of Islamic law. Aurangzeb also ordered the execution of the Sikh guru <a href="/wiki/Guru_Tegh_Bahadur" title="Guru Tegh Bahadur">Tegh Bahadur</a>, leading to the militarization of the Sikh community.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-62"><a href="#cite_note-62">[61]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-AsherTalbot2006-8_56-1"><a href="#cite_note-AsherTalbot2006-8-56">[55]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-BBC_Sikhs_57-1"><a href="#cite_note-BBC_Sikhs-57">[56]</a></sup> From the imperial perspective, conversion to Islam integrated local elites into the king's vision of network of shared identity that would join disparate groups throughout the empire in obedience to the Mughal emperor.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-63"><a href="#cite_note-63">[62]</a></sup> He expanded the empire to include almost the whole of South Asia,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Truschke_61-1"><a href="#cite_note-Truschke-61">[60]</a></sup><sup class="reference nowrap"><span title="Page / location: 1">: 1 </span></sup> but at his death in 1707, "many parts of the empire were in open revolt".<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Berndl_43-10"><a href="#cite_note-Berndl-43">[42]</a></sup> Aurangzeb is considered India's most controversial king,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Truschke_61-2"><a href="#cite_note-Truschke-61">[60]</a></sup> with some historians arguing his religious conservatism and intolerance undermined the stability of Mughal society,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Berndl_43-11"><a href="#cite_note-Berndl-43">[42]</a></sup> while other historians question this, noting that he built <a href="/wiki/Hindu_temple" title="Hindu temple">Hindu temples</a>,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Copland2013_64-0"><a href="#cite_note-Copland2013-64">[63]</a></sup> employed significantly more <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Hindu" title="Hindu">Hindus</a> in his imperial bureaucracy than his predecessors did, opposed bigotry against Hindus and <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Shia_Muslims" title="Shia Muslims">Shia Muslims</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Truschke_61-3"><a href="#cite_note-Truschke-61">[60]</a></sup><sup class="reference nowrap"><span title="Page / location: 58">: 58 </span></sup>
</p>
<h3><span id="Decline_.281707.E2.80.931857.29"></span><span class="mw-headline" id="Decline_(1707–1857)"><span id="Decline">Decline (1707–1857)</span></span></h3>
<link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1033289096" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Further information: <a href="/wiki/Bahadur_Shah_Zafar" title="Bahadur Shah Zafar">Bahadur Shah Zafar</a></div>
<div class="thumb tleft"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Farrukhsiyar_Procession_in_front_of_the_Great_Mosque_of_Delhi.png"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="1500" data-file-width="1006" decoding="async" height="328" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/5/54/Farrukhsiyar_Procession_in_front_of_the_Great_Mosque_of_Delhi.png/220px-Farrukhsiyar_Procession_in_front_of_the_Great_Mosque_of_Delhi.png" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/5/54/Farrukhsiyar_Procession_in_front_of_the_Great_Mosque_of_Delhi.png/330px-Farrukhsiyar_Procession_in_front_of_the_Great_Mosque_of_Delhi.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/5/54/Farrukhsiyar_Procession_in_front_of_the_Great_Mosque_of_Delhi.png/440px-Farrukhsiyar_Procession_in_front_of_the_Great_Mosque_of_Delhi.png 2x" width="220"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Farrukhsiyar_Procession_in_front_of_the_Great_Mosque_of_Delhi.png" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Delhi under the puppet-king <a href="/wiki/Farrukhsiyar" title="Farrukhsiyar">Farrukhsiyar</a></div></div></div>
<div class="thumb tleft"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Equestrian_Portrait_of_Shah_Alam_II,_18th_century.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="1257" data-file-width="960" decoding="async" height="288" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/f/f4/Equestrian_Portrait_of_Shah_Alam_II%2C_18th_century.jpg/220px-Equestrian_Portrait_of_Shah_Alam_II%2C_18th_century.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/f/f4/Equestrian_Portrait_of_Shah_Alam_II%2C_18th_century.jpg/330px-Equestrian_Portrait_of_Shah_Alam_II%2C_18th_century.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/f/f4/Equestrian_Portrait_of_Shah_Alam_II%2C_18th_century.jpg/440px-Equestrian_Portrait_of_Shah_Alam_II%2C_18th_century.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Equestrian_Portrait_of_Shah_Alam_II,_18th_century.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Shah Alam II on horseback</div></div></div>
<p>Aurangzeb's son, <a href="/wiki/Bahadur_Shah_I" title="Bahadur Shah I">Bahadur Shah I</a>, repealed the religious policies of his father and attempted to reform the administration. "However, after his death in 1712, the Mughal dynasty sank into chaos and violent feuds. In 1719 alone, four emperors successively ascended the throne",<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Berndl_43-12"><a href="#cite_note-Berndl-43">[42]</a></sup> as figureheads under the rule of the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Sayyid_Brothers" title="Sayyid Brothers">Sayyid</a> king-makers.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-65"><a href="#cite_note-65">[64]</a></sup>
</p><p>During the reign of <a href="/wiki/Muhammad_Shah" title="Muhammad Shah">Muhammad Shah</a> (reigned 1719–1748), the empire began to break up, and vast tracts of central India passed from Mughal to <a href="/wiki/Maratha_Empire" title="Maratha Empire">Maratha</a> hands. The far-off <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Nadir_Shah%27s_invasion_of_India" title="Nadir Shah's invasion of India">Indian campaign</a> of <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Nadir_Shah" title="Nadir Shah">Nadir Shah</a>, who had previously reestablished Iranian <a href="/wiki/Suzerainty" title="Suzerainty">suzerainty</a> over most of West Asia, the Caucasus, and Central Asia, culminated with the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Sack_of_Delhi" title="Sack of Delhi">Sack of Delhi</a> and shattered the remnants of Mughal power and prestige. Many of the empire's elites now sought to control their own affairs, and broke away to form independent kingdoms.<sup class="noprint Inline-Template Template-Fact" style="white-space:nowrap;">[<i><a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Citation_needed" title="Wikipedia:Citation needed"><span title="This claim needs references to reliable sources. (July 2019)">citation needed</span></a></i>]</sup> As the Mughals tried to suppress the independence of the <a href="/wiki/Nizam-ul-Mulk,_Asaf_Jah_I" title="Nizam-ul-Mulk, Asaf Jah I">Nizam</a> in the Deccan, he encouraged the Marathas to invade central and northern India.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-66"><a href="#cite_note-66">[65]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-67"><a href="#cite_note-67">[66]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-68"><a href="#cite_note-68">[67]</a></sup> But, according to <a href="/wiki/Sugata_Bose" title="Sugata Bose">Sugata Bose</a> and <a href="/wiki/Ayesha_Jalal" title="Ayesha Jalal">Ayesha Jalal</a>, the Mughal Emperor continued to be the highest manifestation of sovereignty. Not only the Muslim gentry, but the Maratha, Hindu, and Sikh leaders took part in ceremonial acknowledgments of the emperor as the sovereign of India.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-MSA2_69-0"><a href="#cite_note-MSA2-69">[68]</a></sup>
</p><p>Meanwhile, some regional polities within the increasingly fragmented Mughal Empire, involved themselves and the state in global conflicts, leading only to defeat and loss of territory during the <a href="/wiki/Carnatic_Wars" title="Carnatic Wars">Carnatic Wars</a> and the <a href="/wiki/Bengal_War" title="Bengal War">Bengal War</a>.
</p>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:1751_map_of_India_from_%22Historical_Atlas_of_India%22,_by_Charles_Joppen.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="1824" data-file-width="1259" decoding="async" height="319" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/c/cf/1751_map_of_India_from_%22Historical_Atlas_of_India%22%2C_by_Charles_Joppen.jpg/220px-1751_map_of_India_from_%22Historical_Atlas_of_India%22%2C_by_Charles_Joppen.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/c/cf/1751_map_of_India_from_%22Historical_Atlas_of_India%22%2C_by_Charles_Joppen.jpg/330px-1751_map_of_India_from_%22Historical_Atlas_of_India%22%2C_by_Charles_Joppen.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/c/cf/1751_map_of_India_from_%22Historical_Atlas_of_India%22%2C_by_Charles_Joppen.jpg/440px-1751_map_of_India_from_%22Historical_Atlas_of_India%22%2C_by_Charles_Joppen.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:1751_map_of_India_from_%22Historical_Atlas_of_India%22,_by_Charles_Joppen.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>The remnants of the empire in 1751</div></div></div>
<p>The Mughal Emperor <a href="/wiki/Shah_Alam_II" title="Shah Alam II">Shah Alam II</a> (1759–1806) made futile attempts to reverse the Mughal decline but ultimately had to seek the protection of the Emir of Afghanistan, Ahmed Shah Abdali, which led to the <a href="/wiki/Third_Battle_of_Panipat" title="Third Battle of Panipat">Third Battle of Panipat</a> between the Maratha Empire and the Afghans (led by Abdali) in 1761. In 1771, the Marathas recaptured Delhi from Afghan control and in 1784 they officially became the protectors of the emperor in Delhi,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Rathod1994_70-0"><a href="#cite_note-Rathod1994-70">[69]</a></sup> a state of affairs that continued until the <a href="/wiki/Second_Anglo-Maratha_War" title="Second Anglo-Maratha War">Second Anglo-Maratha War</a>. Thereafter, the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/British_East_India_Company" title="British East India Company">British East India Company</a> became the protectors of the Mughal dynasty in Delhi.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-MSA2_69-1"><a href="#cite_note-MSA2-69">[68]</a></sup> The British East India <a href="/wiki/Company_rule_in_India" title="Company rule in India">Company</a> took control of the former Mughal province of Bengal-Bihar in 1793 after it abolished local rule (Nizamat) that lasted until 1858, marking the beginning of British colonial era over the Indian subcontinent. By 1857 a considerable part of former Mughal India was under the East India Company's control. After a crushing defeat in the <a href="/wiki/Indian_Rebellion_of_1857" title="Indian Rebellion of 1857">war of 1857–1858</a> which he nominally led, the last Mughal, <a href="/wiki/Bahadur_Shah_Zafar" title="Bahadur Shah Zafar">Bahadur Shah Zafar</a>, was deposed by the British <a href="/wiki/East_India_Company" title="East India Company">East India Company</a> and exiled in 1858. Through the <a href="/wiki/Government_of_India_Act_1858" title="Government of India Act 1858">Government of India Act 1858</a> the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/British_Crown" title="British Crown">British Crown</a> assumed direct control of East India Company-held territories in India in the form of the new <a href="/wiki/British_Raj" title="British Raj">British Raj</a>. In 1876 the British <a href="/wiki/Queen_Victoria" title="Queen Victoria">Queen Victoria</a> assumed the title of <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Empress_of_India" title="Empress of India">Empress of India</a>.
</p>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:172px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Portrait_of_Bahadur_Shah_II_as_calligrapher._Delhi,_ca._1850,_Royal_Ontario_Museum,_Toronto._-_%D0%BA%D0%BE%D0%BF%D0%B8%D1%8F.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="2189" data-file-width="1648" decoding="async" height="226" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/8/87/Portrait_of_Bahadur_Shah_II_as_calligrapher._Delhi%2C_ca._1850%2C_Royal_Ontario_Museum%2C_Toronto._-_%D0%BA%D0%BE%D0%BF%D0%B8%D1%8F.jpg/170px-Portrait_of_Bahadur_Shah_II_as_calligrapher._Delhi%2C_ca._1850%2C_Royal_Ontario_Museum%2C_Toronto._-_%D0%BA%D0%BE%D0%BF%D0%B8%D1%8F.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/8/87/Portrait_of_Bahadur_Shah_II_as_calligrapher._Delhi%2C_ca._1850%2C_Royal_Ontario_Museum%2C_Toronto._-_%D0%BA%D0%BE%D0%BF%D0%B8%D1%8F.jpg/255px-Portrait_of_Bahadur_Shah_II_as_calligrapher._Delhi%2C_ca._1850%2C_Royal_Ontario_Museum%2C_Toronto._-_%D0%BA%D0%BE%D0%BF%D0%B8%D1%8F.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/8/87/Portrait_of_Bahadur_Shah_II_as_calligrapher._Delhi%2C_ca._1850%2C_Royal_Ontario_Museum%2C_Toronto._-_%D0%BA%D0%BE%D0%BF%D0%B8%D1%8F.jpg/340px-Portrait_of_Bahadur_Shah_II_as_calligrapher._Delhi%2C_ca._1850%2C_Royal_Ontario_Museum%2C_Toronto._-_%D0%BA%D0%BE%D0%BF%D0%B8%D1%8F.jpg 2x" width="170"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Portrait_of_Bahadur_Shah_II_as_calligrapher._Delhi,_ca._1850,_Royal_Ontario_Museum,_Toronto._-_%D0%BA%D0%BE%D0%BF%D0%B8%D1%8F.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Portrait of Bahadur Shah II</div></div></div>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Causes_of_decline">Causes of decline</span></h3>
<p>Historians have offered numerous explanations for the rapid collapse of the Mughal Empire between 1707 and 1720, after a century of growth and prosperity. In fiscal terms, the throne lost the revenues needed to pay its chief officers, the emirs (nobles) and their entourages. The emperor lost authority, as the widely scattered imperial officers lost confidence in the central authorities, and made their own deals with local men of influence. The imperial army, bogged down in long, futile wars against the more aggressive <a href="/wiki/Maratha_Empire" title="Maratha Empire">Marathas</a>, lost its fighting spirit. Finally came a series of violent political feuds over control of the throne. After the execution of <a href="/wiki/Farrukhsiyar" title="Farrukhsiyar">Emperor Farrukhsiyar</a> in 1719, local Mughal successor states took power in region after region.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-71"><a href="#cite_note-71">[70]</a></sup>
</p><p>Contemporary chroniclers bewailed the decay they witnessed, a theme picked up by the first British historians who wanted to underscore the need for a British-led rejuvenation.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-72"><a href="#cite_note-72">[71]</a></sup>
</p>
<h4><span class="mw-headline" id="Modern_views_on_the_decline">Modern views on the decline</span></h4>
<p>Since the 1970s historians have taken multiple approaches to the decline, with little consensus on which factor was dominant. The psychological interpretations emphasise depravity in high places, excessive luxury, and increasingly narrow views that left the rulers unprepared for an external challenge. A Marxist school (led by <a href="/wiki/Irfan_Habib" title="Irfan Habib">Irfan Habib</a> and based at <a href="/wiki/Aligarh_Muslim_University" title="Aligarh Muslim University">Aligarh Muslim University</a>) emphasises excessive exploitation of the peasantry by the rich, which stripped away the will and the means to support the regime.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-73"><a href="#cite_note-73">[72]</a></sup> Karen Leonard has focused on the failure of the regime to work with Hindu bankers, whose financial support was increasingly needed; the bankers then helped the Maratha and the British.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-74"><a href="#cite_note-74">[73]</a></sup> In a religious interpretation, some scholars argue that the Hindu powers revolted against the rule of a Muslim dynasty.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-75"><a href="#cite_note-75">[74]</a></sup> Finally, other scholars argue that the very prosperity of the Empire inspired the provinces to achieve a high degree of independence, thus weakening the imperial court.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-76"><a href="#cite_note-76">[75]</a></sup>
</p><p><a href="/wiki/Jeffrey_G._Williamson" title="Jeffrey G. Williamson">Jeffrey G. Williamson</a> has argued that the <a href="/wiki/Economic_history_of_India" title="Economic history of India">Indian economy</a> went through <a href="/wiki/Deindustrialization" title="Deindustrialization">deindustrialization</a> in the latter half of the 18th century as an indirect outcome of the collapse of the Mughal Empire, with <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/British_India" title="British India">British rule</a> later causing further deindustrialization.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-williamson_77-0"><a href="#cite_note-williamson-77">[76]</a></sup> According to Williamson, the decline of the Mughal Empire led to a decline in agricultural productivity, which drove up <a href="/wiki/Food_prices" title="Food prices">food prices</a>, then <a href="/wiki/Real_versus_nominal_value_(economics)" title="Real versus nominal value (economics)">nominal</a> wages, and then textile prices, which led to India losing a share of the world textile market to Britain even before it had superior <a href="/wiki/Industrial_Revolution" title="Industrial Revolution">factory technology</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Jeffrey_G._Williamson_2011_91_78-0"><a href="#cite_note-Jeffrey_G._Williamson_2011_91-78">[77]</a></sup> Indian textiles, however, still maintained a competitive advantage over British textiles up until the 19th century.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-79"><a href="#cite_note-79">[78]</a></sup>
</p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Administration_and_state">Administration and state</span></h2>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Government">Government</span></h3>
<p>The Mughal Empire had a highly centralised, bureaucratic government, most of which was instituted during the rule of the third Mughal emperor Akbar.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-:4_80-0"><a href="#cite_note-:4-80">[79]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-81"><a href="#cite_note-81">[80]</a></sup>  The central government was headed by the Mughal emperor; immediately beneath him were four ministries. The finance/revenue ministry was responsible for controlling revenues from the empire's territories, calculating tax revenues, and using this information to distribute assignments. The ministry of the military (army/intelligence) was headed by an official titled <i>mir bakhshi</i>, who was in charge of military organisation, messenger service, and the <i><a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Mansabdari" title="Mansabdari">mansabdari</a></i> system. The ministry in charge of law/religious patronage was the responsibility of the <i>sadr as-sudr,</i> who appointed judges and managed charities and stipends. Another ministry was dedicated to the imperial household and public works.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-:3_82-0"><a href="#cite_note-:3-82">[81]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-:4_80-1"><a href="#cite_note-:4-80">[79]</a></sup>
</p><p>The empire was divided into <i><a href="/wiki/Subah" title="Subah">suba</a></i> (provinces), each of which were headed by a provincial governor called a <i><a href="/wiki/Subahdar" title="Subahdar">subadar</a>.</i> The structure of the central government was mirrored at the provincial level; each <i>suba</i> had its own <i>bakhshi</i>, <i>sadr as-sudr</i>, and finance minister that reported directly to the central government rather than the <i>subahdar</i>. <i>Subas</i> were subdivided into administrative units known as <i><a href="/wiki/Sarkar_(administrative_division)" title="Sarkar (administrative division)">sarkars</a>,</i> which were further divided into groups of villages known as <i><a href="/wiki/Pargana" title="Pargana">parganas</a>.</i> Mughal government in the <i>pargana</i> consisted of a Muslim judge and local tax collector.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-:3_82-1"><a href="#cite_note-:3-82">[81]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-:4_80-2"><a href="#cite_note-:4-80">[79]</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Capitals">Capitals</span></h3>
<p>The Mughals had multiple imperial capitals, established over the course of their rule. These were the cities of <a href="/wiki/Agra" title="Agra">Agra</a>, <a href="/wiki/Delhi" title="Delhi">Delhi</a>, <a href="/wiki/Lahore" title="Lahore">Lahore</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Fatehpur_Sikri" title="Fatehpur Sikri">Fatehpur Sikri</a>. Power often shifted back and forth between these capitals.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-:1_83-0"><a href="#cite_note-:1-83">[82]</a></sup> Sometimes this was necessitated by political and military demands, but shifts also occurred for ideological reasons (for example, Akbar's establishment of Fatehpur Sikri), or even simply because the cost of establishing a new capital was marginal.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-84"><a href="#cite_note-84">[83]</a></sup> Situations where there were two simultaneous capitals happened multiple times in Mughal history. Certain cities also served as short-term, provincial capitals, as was the case with Aurangzeb's shift to <a href="/wiki/Aurangabad" title="Aurangabad">Aurangabad</a> in the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Deccan" title="Deccan">Deccan</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-:1_83-1"><a href="#cite_note-:1-83">[82]</a></sup>
</p><p>The imperial camp, used for military expeditions and royal tours, also served as a kind of mobile, "de-facto" administrative capital. From the time of Akbar, Mughal camps were huge in scale, accompanied by numerous personages associated with the royal court, as well as soldiers and labourers. All administration and governance was carried out within them. The Mughal Emperors spent a significant portion of their ruling period within these camps.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-85"><a href="#cite_note-85">[84]</a></sup>
</p><p>After Aurangzeb, the Mughal capital definitively became the walled city of <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Shahjahanabad" title="Shahjahanabad">Shahjahanabad</a> (today Old Delhi).<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-86"><a href="#cite_note-86">[85]</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Law">Law</span></h3>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Police_Chuprasi,_Delhi.png"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="865" data-file-width="652" decoding="async" height="292" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/1/14/Police_Chuprasi%2C_Delhi.png/220px-Police_Chuprasi%2C_Delhi.png" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/1/14/Police_Chuprasi%2C_Delhi.png/330px-Police_Chuprasi%2C_Delhi.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/1/14/Police_Chuprasi%2C_Delhi.png/440px-Police_Chuprasi%2C_Delhi.png 2x" width="220"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Police_Chuprasi,_Delhi.png" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Police in Delhi under Bahadur Shah II, 1842</div></div></div>
<p>The Mughal Empire's legal system was context-specific and evolved over the course of the empire's rule. Being a Muslim state, the empire employed <i><a href="/wiki/Fiqh" title="Fiqh">fiqh</a></i> (Islamic jurisprudence) and therefore the fundamental institutions of Islamic law such as those of the <i><a href="/wiki/Qadi" title="Qadi">qadi</a></i> (judge), <i><a href="/wiki/Mufti" title="Mufti">mufti</a></i> (jurisconsult), and <i><a href="/wiki/Muhtasib" title="Muhtasib">muhtasib</a></i> (censor and market supervisor) were well-established in the Mughal Empire. However, the dispensation of justice also depended on other factors, such as administrative rules, local customs, and political convenience. This was due to Persianate influences on Mughal ideology, and the fact that the Mughal Empire governed a non-Muslim majority.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-:2_87-0"><a href="#cite_note-:2-87">[86]</a></sup>
</p>
<h4><span class="mw-headline" id="Legal_ideology">Legal ideology</span></h4>
<p>The Mughal Empire followed the Sunni <a href="/wiki/Hanafi" title="Hanafi">Hanafi</a> system of jurisprudence. In its early years, the empire relied on Hanafi legal references inherited from its predecessor, the Delhi Sultanate. These included the <i><a href="/wiki/Al-Hidayah" title="Al-Hidayah">al-Hidaya</a></i> (the best guidance) and the <i>Fatawa al-Tatarkhaniyya</i> (religious decisions of the Emire Tatarkhan). During the Mughal Empire's peak, the <i><a href="/wiki/Fatawa_%27Alamgiri" title="Fatawa 'Alamgiri">Al-Fatawa al-'Alamgiriyya</a></i> was commissioned by Emperor Aurangzeb. This compendium of Hanafi law sought to serve as a central reference for the Mughal state that dealt with the specifics of the South Asian context.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-88"><a href="#cite_note-88">[87]</a></sup>
</p><p>The Mughal Empire also drew on Persianate notions of kingship. Particularly, this meant that the Mughal emperor was considered the supreme authority on legal affairs.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-:2_87-1"><a href="#cite_note-:2-87">[86]</a></sup>
</p>
<h4><span class="mw-headline" id="Courts_of_law">Courts of law</span></h4>
<p>Various kinds of courts existed in the Mughal empire. One such court was that of the <i>qadi</i>. The Mughal <i>qadi</i> was responsible for dispensing justice; this included settling disputes, judging people for crimes, and dealing with inheritances and orphans. The <i>qadi</i> also had additional importance with regards to documents, as the seal of the <i>qadi</i> was required to validate deeds and tax records. <i>Qadis</i> did not constitute a single position, but made up a hierarchy. For example, the most basic kind was the <i><a href="/wiki/Pargana" title="Pargana">pargana</a></i> (district) <i>qadi</i>. More prestigious positions were those of the <i>qadi al-quddat</i> (judge of judges) who accompanied the mobile imperial camp, and the <i>qadi-yi lashkar</i> (judge of the army).<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-:2_87-2"><a href="#cite_note-:2-87">[86]</a></sup> <i>Qadis</i> were usually appointed by the emperor or the <i>sadr-us-sudr</i> (chief of charities).<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-:2_87-3"><a href="#cite_note-:2-87">[86]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-89"><a href="#cite_note-89">[88]</a></sup> The jurisdiction of the <i>qadi</i> was availed by Muslims and non-Muslims alike.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-90"><a href="#cite_note-90">[89]</a></sup>
</p><p>The <i><a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Jagirdar" title="Jagirdar">jagirdar</a></i> (local tax collector) was another kind of official approached, especially for high-stakes cases. Subjects of the Mughal Empire also took their grievances to the courts of superior officials who held more authority and punitive power than the local <i>qadi</i>. Such officials included the <i><a href="/wiki/Kotwal" title="Kotwal">kotwal</a></i> (local police), the <i><a href="/wiki/Faujdar" title="Faujdar">faujdar</a></i> (an officer controlling multiple districts and troops of soldiers), and the most powerful, the <i><a href="/wiki/Subahdar" title="Subahdar">subahdar</a></i> (provincial governor). In some cases, the emperor themself dispensed justice directly.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-:2_87-4"><a href="#cite_note-:2-87">[86]</a></sup> Jahangir was known to have installed a "chain of justice" in the <a href="/wiki/Agra_Fort" title="Agra Fort">Agra fort</a> that any aggrieved subject could shake to get the attention of the emperor and bypass the inefficacy of officials.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-91"><a href="#cite_note-91">[90]</a></sup>
</p><p>Self-regulating tribunals operating at the community or village level were common, but sparse documentation of them exists. For example, it is unclear how <i><a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Panchayats" title="Panchayats">panchayats</a></i> (village councils) operated in the Mughal era.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-:2_87-5"><a href="#cite_note-:2-87">[86]</a></sup>
</p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Economy">Economy</span></h2>
<link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1033289096" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">See also: <a href="/wiki/Economic_history_of_India" title="Economic history of India">Economic history of India</a> and <a href="/wiki/Economic_history_of_the_Indian_subcontinent" title="Economic history of the Indian subcontinent">Economic history of the Indian subcontinent</a></div>
<p>The Indian economy was large and prosperous under the Mughal Empire.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Schmidt2015_92-0"><a href="#cite_note-Schmidt2015-92">[91]</a></sup> During the Mughal era, the <a href="/wiki/Gross_domestic_product" title="Gross domestic product">gross domestic product</a> (GDP) of India in 1600 was estimated at 22% of the <a href="/wiki/World_economy" title="World economy">world economy</a>, the second largest in the world, behind only <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Ming_China" title="Ming China">Ming China</a> but larger than Europe. By 1700, the GDP of Mughal India had risen to 24% of the world economy, the largest in the world, larger than both <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Qing_China" title="Qing China">Qing China</a> and Western Europe.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Maddison2003_93-0"><a href="#cite_note-Maddison2003-93">[92]</a></sup> The Mughal empire was producing about 25% of the world's industrial output up until the 18th century.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Williamson_94-0"><a href="#cite_note-Williamson-94">[93]</a></sup> India's GDP growth increased under the Mughal Empire, with India's GDP having a faster growth rate during the Mughal era than in the 1,500 years prior to the Mughal era.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Maddison2003_93-1"><a href="#cite_note-Maddison2003-93">[92]</a></sup> Mughal India's economy has been described as a form of <a href="/wiki/Proto-industrialization" title="Proto-industrialization">proto-industrialization</a>, like that of 18th-century Western Europe prior to the <a href="/wiki/Industrial_Revolution" title="Industrial Revolution">Industrial Revolution</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-voss_95-0"><a href="#cite_note-voss-95">[94]</a></sup>
</p><p>The Mughals were responsible for building an extensive road system, creating a uniform currency, and the unification of the country.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Richards1995_11-1"><a href="#cite_note-Richards1995-11">[10]</a></sup><sup class="reference nowrap"><span title="Page / location: 185–204">: 185–204 </span></sup> The empire had an extensive road network, which was vital to the economic infrastructure, built by a <a href="/wiki/Public_works" title="Public works">public works</a> department set up by the Mughals which designed, constructed and maintained roads linking towns and cities across the empire, making trade easier to conduct.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Schmidt2015_92-1"><a href="#cite_note-Schmidt2015-92">[91]</a></sup>
</p><p>The main base of the empire's collective wealth was agricultural taxes, instituted by the third Mughal emperor, Akbar.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Stein2010-3_24-1"><a href="#cite_note-Stein2010-3-24">[23]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-AsherTalbot2006-1_25-1"><a href="#cite_note-AsherTalbot2006-1-25">[24]</a></sup> These taxes, which amounted to well over half the output of a peasant cultivator,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Stein2010-4_26-1"><a href="#cite_note-Stein2010-4-26">[25]</a></sup> were paid in the well-regulated silver currency,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-AsherTalbot2006-2_23-2"><a href="#cite_note-AsherTalbot2006-2-23">[22]</a></sup> and caused peasants and artisans to enter larger markets.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-AsherTalbot2006-3_27-1"><a href="#cite_note-AsherTalbot2006-3-27">[26]</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Coinage">Coinage</span></h3>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Coin_of_Aurangzeb,_minted_in_Kabul.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="393" data-file-width="800" decoding="async" height="108" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/4/4d/Coin_of_Aurangzeb%2C_minted_in_Kabul.jpg/220px-Coin_of_Aurangzeb%2C_minted_in_Kabul.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/4/4d/Coin_of_Aurangzeb%2C_minted_in_Kabul.jpg/330px-Coin_of_Aurangzeb%2C_minted_in_Kabul.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/4/4d/Coin_of_Aurangzeb%2C_minted_in_Kabul.jpg/440px-Coin_of_Aurangzeb%2C_minted_in_Kabul.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Coin_of_Aurangzeb,_minted_in_Kabul.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Coin of Aurangzeb, minted in Kabul, dated 1691/2</div></div></div>
<p>The Mughals adopted and standardised the <a href="/wiki/Rupee" title="Rupee">rupee</a> (<i>rupiya</i>, or silver) and <a href="/wiki/Dam_(Indian_coin)" title="Dam (Indian coin)">dam</a> (copper) currencies introduced by <a href="/wiki/Sur_Empire" title="Sur Empire">Sur</a> Emperor <a href="/wiki/Sher_Shah_Suri" title="Sher Shah Suri">Sher Shah Suri</a> during his brief rule.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-96"><a href="#cite_note-96">[95]</a></sup> The currency was initially 48 dams to a single rupee in the beginning of Akbar's reign, before it later became 38 dams to a rupee in the 1580s, with the dam's value rising further in the 17th century as a result of new industrial uses for copper, such as in bronze cannons and brass utensils. The dam was initially the most common coin in Akbar's time, before being replaced by the rupee as the most common coin in succeeding reigns.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Richards1995_11-2"><a href="#cite_note-Richards1995-11">[10]</a></sup> The dam's value was later worth 30 to a rupee towards the end of Jahangir's reign, and then 16 to a rupee by the 1660s.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-97"><a href="#cite_note-97">[96]</a></sup> The Mughals minted coins with high purity, never dropping below 96%, and without <a href="/wiki/Debasement" title="Debasement">debasement</a> until the 1720s.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Richards2003_98-0"><a href="#cite_note-Richards2003-98">[97]</a></sup>
</p><p>Despite India having its own stocks of gold and silver, the Mughals produced minimal gold of their own, but mostly minted coins from imported <a href="/wiki/Bullion" title="Bullion">bullion</a>, as a result of the empire's strong export-driven economy, with global demand for Indian agricultural and industrial products drawing a steady stream of <a href="/wiki/Precious_metal" title="Precious metal">precious metals</a> into India.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Richards1995_11-3"><a href="#cite_note-Richards1995-11">[10]</a></sup> Around 80% of Mughal India's imports were bullion, mostly silver,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Tracy1997_99-0"><a href="#cite_note-Tracy1997-99">[98]</a></sup> with major sources of imported bullion including the <a href="/wiki/New_World" title="New World">New World</a> and Japan,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Richards2003_98-1"><a href="#cite_note-Richards2003-98">[97]</a></sup> which in turn imported large quantities of textiles and silk from the <a href="/wiki/Bengal_Subah" title="Bengal Subah">Bengal Subah</a> province.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Richards1995_11-4"><a href="#cite_note-Richards1995-11">[10]</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Labour">Labour</span></h3>
<p>The historian Shireen Moosvi estimates that in terms of contributions to the Mughal economy, in the late 16th century, the primary sector contributed 52%, the secondary sector 18% and the tertiary sector 29%; the secondary sector contributed a higher percentage than in early 20th-century <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/British_India" title="British India">British India</a>, where the secondary sector only contributed 11% to the economy.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEMoosvi2015433_100-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEMoosvi2015433-100">[99]</a></sup> In terms of urban-rural divide, 18% of Mughal India's labour force were urban and 82% were rural, contributing 52% and 48% to the economy, respectively.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-101"><a href="#cite_note-101">[100]</a></sup>
</p><p>According to Stephen Broadberry and Bishnupriya Gupta, grain wages in India were comparable to England in the 16th and 17th centuries, but diverged in the 18th century when they fell to 20-40% of England's wages.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-102"><a href="#cite_note-102">[101]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-103"><a href="#cite_note-103">[102]</a></sup> This, however, is disputed by Parthasarathi and Sivramkrishna. Parthasarathi cites his estimates that grain wages for weaving and spinning in mid-18 century Bengal and South India was comparable to Britain.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-104"><a href="#cite_note-104">[103]</a></sup> Similarly, Sivramkrishna analyzed agricultural surveys conducted in Mysore by Francis Buchanan during 1800–1801, arrived at estimates using a "subsistence basket" that aggregated <a href="/wiki/Millet" title="Millet">millet</a> income could be almost five times subsistence level, while corresponding rice income was three times that much.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-105"><a href="#cite_note-105">[104]</a></sup> That could be comparable to advance part of Europe.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-106"><a href="#cite_note-106">[105]</a></sup> Due to the scarcity of data, however, more research is needed before drawing any conclusion.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-107"><a href="#cite_note-107">[106]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-108"><a href="#cite_note-108">[107]</a></sup>
</p><p>According to Moosvi, Mughal India had a per-capita income, in terms of wheat, 1.24% higher in the late 16th century than British India did in the early 20th century.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEMoosvi2015432_109-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEMoosvi2015432-109">[108]</a></sup> This income, however, would have to be revised downwards if manufactured goods, like clothing, would be considered. Compared to food per-capita, expenditure on clothing was much smaller though, so relative income between 1595 and 1596 should be comparable to 1901–1910.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEMoosvi2015450_110-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEMoosvi2015450-110">[109]</a></sup> However, in a system where wealth was hoarded by elites, wages were depressed for <a href="/wiki/Manual_labour" title="Manual labour">manual labour</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-moosvi_111-0"><a href="#cite_note-moosvi-111">[110]</a></sup> In Mughal India, there was a generally tolerant attitude towards manual labourers, with some religious cults in northern India proudly asserting a high status for manual labour. While <a href="/wiki/Slavery" title="Slavery">slavery</a> also existed, it was limited largely to household servants.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-moosvi_111-1"><a href="#cite_note-moosvi-111">[110]</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Agriculture">Agriculture</span></h3>
<p>Indian agricultural production increased under the Mughal Empire.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Schmidt2015_92-2"><a href="#cite_note-Schmidt2015-92">[91]</a></sup> A variety of crops were grown, including food crops such as wheat, rice, and <a href="/wiki/Barley" title="Barley">barley</a>, and non-food <a href="/wiki/Cash_crop" title="Cash crop">cash crops</a> such as cotton, <a href="/wiki/Indigofera_tinctoria" title="Indigofera tinctoria">indigo</a> and <a href="/wiki/Opium" title="Opium">opium</a>. By the mid-17th century, Indian cultivators begun to extensively grow two new crops from the Americas, maize and tobacco.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Schmidt2015_92-3"><a href="#cite_note-Schmidt2015-92">[91]</a></sup>
</p><p>The Mughal administration emphasised <a href="/wiki/Agrarian_reform" title="Agrarian reform">agrarian reform</a>, which began under the non-Mughal emperor Sher Shah Suri, the work of which Akbar adopted and furthered with more reforms. The civil administration was organised in a hierarchical manner on the basis of merit, with promotions based on performance.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-112"><a href="#cite_note-112">[111]</a></sup> The Mughal government funded the building of <a href="/wiki/Irrigation" title="Irrigation">irrigation</a> systems across the empire, which produced much higher <a href="/wiki/Crop_yield" title="Crop yield">crop yields</a> and increased the net revenue base, leading to increased agricultural production.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Schmidt2015_92-4"><a href="#cite_note-Schmidt2015-92">[91]</a></sup>
</p><p>A major Mughal reform introduced by Akbar was a new land revenue system called <i>zabt</i>. He replaced the <a href="/wiki/Tribute" title="Tribute">tribute</a> system, previously common in India and used by <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Tokugawa_Japan" title="Tokugawa Japan">Tokugawa Japan</a> at the time, with a monetary tax system based on a uniform currency.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Richards2003_98-2"><a href="#cite_note-Richards2003-98">[97]</a></sup> The revenue system was biased in favour of higher value cash crops such as cotton, indigo, <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Sugar_cane" title="Sugar cane">sugar cane</a>, tree-crops, and opium, providing state incentives to grow cash crops, in addition to rising market demand.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Richards1995_11-5"><a href="#cite_note-Richards1995-11">[10]</a></sup> Under the <i>zabt</i> system, the Mughals also conducted extensive <a href="/wiki/Cadastral_surveying" title="Cadastral surveying">cadastral surveying</a> to assess the area of land under <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Plow" title="Plow">plow</a> cultivation, with the Mughal state encouraging greater land cultivation by offering tax-free periods to those who brought new land under cultivation.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Richards2003_98-3"><a href="#cite_note-Richards2003-98">[97]</a></sup> The expansion of agriculture and cultivation continued under later Mughal emperors including Aurangzeb, whose 1665 <a href="/wiki/Firman" title="Firman">firman</a> edict stated: "the entire elevated attention and desires of the Emperor are devoted to the increase in the population and cultivation of the Empire and the welfare of the whole peasantry and the entire people."<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Ludden96_113-0"><a href="#cite_note-Ludden96-113">[112]</a></sup>
</p><p>Mughal agriculture was in some ways advanced compared to European agriculture at the time, exemplified by the common use of the <a href="/wiki/Seed_drill" title="Seed drill">seed drill</a> among Indian peasants before its adoption in Europe.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-114"><a href="#cite_note-114">[113]</a></sup> While the average peasant across the world was only skilled in growing very few crops, the average Indian peasant was skilled in growing a wide variety of food and non-food crops, increasing their productivity.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-115"><a href="#cite_note-115">[114]</a></sup> Indian peasants were also quick to adapt to profitable new crops, such as maize and tobacco from the New World being rapidly adopted and widely cultivated across Mughal India between 1600 and 1650. <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Bengali_people" title="Bengali people">Bengali</a> farmers rapidly learned techniques of <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Mulberry" title="Mulberry">mulberry</a> cultivation and <a href="/wiki/Sericulture" title="Sericulture">sericulture</a>, establishing <a href="/wiki/Bengal_Subah" title="Bengal Subah">Bengal Subah</a> as a major silk-producing region of the world.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Richards1995_11-6"><a href="#cite_note-Richards1995-11">[10]</a></sup> <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Sugar_mill" title="Sugar mill">Sugar mills</a> appeared in India shortly before the Mughal era. Evidence for the use of a <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Draw_bar" title="Draw bar">draw bar</a> for sugar-milling appears at Delhi in 1540, but may also date back earlier, and was mainly used in the northern Indian subcontinent. Geared sugar <a href="/wiki/Roller_mill" title="Roller mill">rolling mills</a> first appeared in Mughal India, using the principle of rollers as well as <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Worm_gear" title="Worm gear">worm gearing</a>, by the 17th century.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-K8kO4J3mXUAC_page_53_116-0"><a href="#cite_note-K8kO4J3mXUAC_page_53-116">[115]</a></sup>
</p><p>According to economic historian <a href="/wiki/Immanuel_Wallerstein" title="Immanuel Wallerstein">Immanuel Wallerstein</a>, citing evidence from <a href="/wiki/Irfan_Habib" title="Irfan Habib">Irfan Habib</a>, <a href="/wiki/Percival_Spear" title="Percival Spear">Percival Spear</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Ashok_Desai" title="Ashok Desai">Ashok Desai</a>, per-capita agricultural output and standards of consumption in 17th-century <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Mughal_India" title="Mughal India">Mughal India</a> were probably higher than in 17th-century Europe and certainly higher than early 20th-century <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/British_India" title="British India">British India</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-117"><a href="#cite_note-117">[116]</a></sup> The increased agricultural productivity led to lower food prices. In turn, this benefited the Indian <a href="/wiki/Textile_industry" title="Textile industry">textile industry</a>. Compared to Britain, the price of grain was about one-half in South India and one-third in Bengal, in terms of silver coinage. This resulted in lower silver coin prices for Indian textiles, giving them a price advantage in global markets.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Parthasarathi38_118-0"><a href="#cite_note-Parthasarathi38-118">[117]</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Industrial_manufacturing">Industrial manufacturing</span></h3>
<p>Up until 1750, India produced about 25% of the world's industrial output.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-williamson_77-1"><a href="#cite_note-williamson-77">[76]</a></sup> <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Manufactured_goods" title="Manufactured goods">Manufactured goods</a> and cash crops from the Mughal Empire were sold throughout the world. Key industries included textiles, <a href="/wiki/Shipbuilding" title="Shipbuilding">shipbuilding</a>, and steel. Processed products included cotton textiles, <a href="/wiki/Yarn" title="Yarn">yarns</a>, <a href="/wiki/Thread_(yarn)" title="Thread (yarn)">thread</a>, silk, <a href="/wiki/Jute" title="Jute">jute</a> products, <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Metalware" title="Metalware">metalware</a>, and foods such as sugar, oils and butter.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Schmidt2015_92-5"><a href="#cite_note-Schmidt2015-92">[91]</a></sup> The growth of manufacturing industries in the Indian subcontinent during the Mughal era in the 17th–18th centuries has been referred to as a form of <a href="/wiki/Proto-industrialization" title="Proto-industrialization">proto-industrialization</a>, similar to 18th-century Western Europe prior to the <a href="/wiki/Industrial_Revolution" title="Industrial Revolution">Industrial Revolution</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-voss_95-1"><a href="#cite_note-voss-95">[94]</a></sup>
</p><p>In <a href="/wiki/Early_modern_Europe" title="Early modern Europe">early modern Europe</a>, there was significant demand for products from Mughal India, particularly cotton textiles, as well as goods such as spices, peppers, <a href="/wiki/Indigo" title="Indigo">indigo</a>, silks, and <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Saltpeter" title="Saltpeter">saltpeter</a> (for use in <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Munitions" title="Munitions">munitions</a>).<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Schmidt2015_92-6"><a href="#cite_note-Schmidt2015-92">[91]</a></sup> <a href="/wiki/1650%E2%80%931700_in_Western_European_fashion" title="1650–1700 in Western European fashion">European fashion</a>, for example, became increasingly dependent on Mughal Indian textiles and silks. From the late 17th century to the early 18th century, Mughal India accounted for 95% of <a href="/wiki/East_India_Company" title="East India Company">British imports</a> from Asia, and the <a href="/wiki/Bengal_Subah" title="Bengal Subah">Bengal Subah</a> province alone accounted for 40% of <a href="/wiki/Dutch_East_India_Company" title="Dutch East India Company">Dutch imports</a> from Asia.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Prakash_119-0"><a href="#cite_note-Prakash-119">[118]</a></sup> In contrast, there was very little demand for European goods in Mughal India, which was largely self-sufficient, thus Europeans had very little to offer, except for some <a href="/wiki/Woolen" title="Woolen">woolens</a>, unprocessed metals and a few luxury items. The trade imbalance caused Europeans to export large quantities of gold and silver to Mughal India in order to pay for South Asian imports.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Schmidt2015_92-7"><a href="#cite_note-Schmidt2015-92">[91]</a></sup> Indian goods, especially those from Bengal, were also exported in large quantities to other Asian markets, such as <a href="/wiki/Indonesia" title="Indonesia">Indonesia</a> and Japan.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Richards1995_11-7"><a href="#cite_note-Richards1995-11">[10]</a></sup>
</p>
<h4><span class="mw-headline" id="Textile_industry">Textile industry</span></h4>
<link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1033289096" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">See also: <a href="/wiki/Muslin_trade_in_Bengal" title="Muslin trade in Bengal">Muslin trade in Bengal</a> and <a href="/wiki/Mughal_clothing" title="Mughal clothing">Mughal clothing</a></div>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Old_Mughal_Courtier.png"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="824" data-file-width="556" decoding="async" height="326" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/4/42/Old_Mughal_Courtier.png/220px-Old_Mughal_Courtier.png" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/4/42/Old_Mughal_Courtier.png/330px-Old_Mughal_Courtier.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/4/42/Old_Mughal_Courtier.png/440px-Old_Mughal_Courtier.png 2x" width="220"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Old_Mughal_Courtier.png" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Miniature painting - Portrait of an Old Mughal Courtier Wearing Muslin</div></div></div>
