<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Renaldis_muslin_woman.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="4675" data-file-width="5876" decoding="async" height="175" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/f/f2/Renaldis_muslin_woman.jpg/220px-Renaldis_muslin_woman.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/f/f2/Renaldis_muslin_woman.jpg/330px-Renaldis_muslin_woman.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/f/f2/Renaldis_muslin_woman.jpg/440px-Renaldis_muslin_woman.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Renaldis_muslin_woman.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Muslim Lady Reclining or An Indian Girl with a Hookah, painted in Dacca, 18th century</div></div></div>
<p>The largest manufacturing industry in the Mughal Empire was <a href="/wiki/Textile_manufacturing" title="Textile manufacturing">textile manufacturing</a>, particularly cotton textile manufacturing, which included the production of <a href="/wiki/Piece_goods" title="Piece goods">piece goods</a>, <a href="/wiki/Calico" title="Calico">calicos</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Muslin" title="Muslin">muslins</a>, available unbleached and in a variety of colours. The cotton <a href="/wiki/Textile_industry" title="Textile industry">textile industry</a> was responsible for a large part of the empire's international trade.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Schmidt2015_92-8"><a href="#cite_note-Schmidt2015-92">[91]</a></sup> India had a 25% share of the global textile trade in the early 18th century.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-120"><a href="#cite_note-120">[119]</a></sup> Indian cotton textiles were the most important manufactured goods in world trade in the 18th century, consumed across the world from the Americas to Japan.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Parthasarathi_121-0"><a href="#cite_note-Parthasarathi-121">[120]</a></sup> By the early 18th century, Mughal Indian textiles were clothing people across the Indian subcontinent, Southeast Asia, Europe, the Americas, Africa, and the Middle East.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Jeffrey_G._Williamson_2011_91_78-1"><a href="#cite_note-Jeffrey_G._Williamson_2011_91-78">[77]</a></sup> The most important centre of cotton production was the Bengal province, particularly around its capital city of <a href="/wiki/Dhaka" title="Dhaka">Dhaka</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Eaton_122-0"><a href="#cite_note-Eaton-122">[121]</a></sup>
</p><p>Bengal accounted for more than 50% of textiles and around 80% of silks imported by the Dutch from Asia,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Prakash_119-1"><a href="#cite_note-Prakash-119">[118]</a></sup> Bengali silk and cotton textiles were exported in large quantities to Europe, Indonesia, and Japan,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Richards1995_11-8"><a href="#cite_note-Richards1995-11">[10]</a></sup><sup class="reference nowrap"><span title="Page / location: 202">: 202 </span></sup> and <a href="/wiki/Muslin_trade_in_Bengal" title="Muslin trade in Bengal">Bengali muslin</a> textiles from Dhaka were sold in Central Asia, where they were known as "daka" textiles.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Eaton_122-1"><a href="#cite_note-Eaton-122">[121]</a></sup> Indian textiles dominated the <a href="/wiki/Indian_Ocean_trade" title="Indian Ocean trade">Indian Ocean trade</a> for centuries, were sold in the Atlantic Ocean trade, and had a 38% share of the West African trade in the early 18th century, while Indian calicos were a major force in Europe, and Indian textiles accounted for 20% of total English trade with Southern Europe in the early 18th century.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-williamson_77-2"><a href="#cite_note-williamson-77">[76]</a></sup>
</p><p>The <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Worm_gear" title="Worm gear">worm gear</a> roller <a href="/wiki/Cotton_gin" title="Cotton gin">cotton gin</a>, which was invented in India during the early <a href="/wiki/Delhi_Sultanate" title="Delhi Sultanate">Delhi Sultanate</a> era of the 13th–14th centuries, came into use in the Mughal Empire sometime around the 16th century,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-K8kO4J3mXUAC_page_53_116-1"><a href="#cite_note-K8kO4J3mXUAC_page_53-116">[115]</a></sup> and is still used in India through to the present day.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-LakGin_123-0"><a href="#cite_note-LakGin-123">[122]</a></sup> Another innovation, the incorporation of the <a href="/wiki/Crank_(mechanism)" title="Crank (mechanism)">crank</a> handle in the cotton gin, first appeared in India sometime during the late Delhi Sultanate or the early Mughal Empire.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-124"><a href="#cite_note-124">[123]</a></sup> The production of cotton, which may have largely been spun in the villages and then taken to towns in the form of yarn to be woven into cloth textiles, was advanced by the diffusion of the <a href="/wiki/Spinning_wheel" title="Spinning wheel">spinning wheel</a> across India shortly before the Mughal era, lowering the costs of yarn and helping to increase demand for cotton. The diffusion of the spinning wheel, and the incorporation of the worm gear and crank handle into the roller cotton gin led to greatly expanded Indian cotton textile production during the Mughal era.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-125"><a href="#cite_note-125">[124]</a></sup>
</p>
<blockquote><p> Once, the Mughal emperor Akbar asked his courtiers, which was the most beautiful flower. Some said rose, from whose petals were distilled the precious itr, others, the lotus, glory of every Indian village. But Birbal said, “The cotton boll”. There was a scornful laughter and Akbar asked for an explanation. Birbal said, “Your Majesty, from the cotton boll comes the fine fabric prized by merchants across the seas that has made your empire famous throughout the world. The perfume of your fame far exceeds the scent of roses and jasmine. That is why I say the cotton boll is the most beautiful flower.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-126"><a href="#cite_note-126">[125]</a></sup></p></blockquote>
<h4><span class="mw-headline" id="Shipbuilding_industry">Shipbuilding industry</span></h4>
<p>Mughal India had a large <a href="/wiki/Shipbuilding" title="Shipbuilding">shipbuilding</a> industry, which was also largely centred in the Bengal province. Economic historian Indrajit Ray estimates shipbuilding output of Bengal during the sixteenth and seventeenth centuries at 223,250 tons annually, compared with 23,061 tons produced in nineteen colonies in North America from 1769 to 1771.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-ray174_127-0"><a href="#cite_note-ray174-127">[126]</a></sup> He also assesses ship repairing as very advanced in Bengal.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-ray174_127-1"><a href="#cite_note-ray174-127">[126]</a></sup>
</p><p>Indian shipbuilding, particularly in Bengal, was advanced compared to European shipbuilding at the time, with Indians selling ships to European firms. An important innovation in shipbuilding was the introduction of a <a href="/wiki/Flush_deck" title="Flush deck">flushed deck</a> design in Bengal rice ships, resulting in <a href="/wiki/Hull_(watercraft)" title="Hull (watercraft)">hulls</a> that were stronger and less prone to leak than the structurally weak hulls of traditional European ships built with a stepped <a href="/wiki/Deck_(ship)" title="Deck (ship)">deck</a> design. The British <a href="/wiki/East_India_Company" title="East India Company">East India Company</a> later duplicated the flushed deck and hull designs of Bengal rice ships in the 1760s, leading to significant improvements in <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Seaworthiness" title="Seaworthiness">seaworthiness</a> and navigation for European ships during the <a href="/wiki/Industrial_Revolution" title="Industrial Revolution">Industrial Revolution</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-:0_128-0"><a href="#cite_note-:0-128">[127]</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Bengal_Subah">Bengal Subah</span></h3>
<link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1033289096" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Bengal_Subah" title="Bengal Subah">Bengal Subah</a></div>
<link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1033289096" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">See also: <a href="/wiki/Muslin_trade_in_Bengal" title="Muslin trade in Bengal">Muslin trade in Bengal</a></div>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Boro_Katra_4_by_Ashif_Siddique.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="1536" data-file-width="2048" decoding="async" height="165" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/2/24/Boro_Katra_4_by_Ashif_Siddique.jpg/220px-Boro_Katra_4_by_Ashif_Siddique.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/2/24/Boro_Katra_4_by_Ashif_Siddique.jpg/330px-Boro_Katra_4_by_Ashif_Siddique.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/2/24/Boro_Katra_4_by_Ashif_Siddique.jpg/440px-Boro_Katra_4_by_Ashif_Siddique.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Boro_Katra_4_by_Ashif_Siddique.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Ruins of the <a href="/wiki/Bara_Katra" title="Bara Katra">Great Caravanserai</a> in <a href="/wiki/Dhaka" title="Dhaka">Dhaka</a>.</div></div></div>
<p>The Bengal Subah province was especially prosperous from the time of its takeover by the Mughals in 1590 until the British East India Company seized control in 1757.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-129"><a href="#cite_note-129">[128]</a></sup> It was the Mughal Empire's wealthiest province.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-130"><a href="#cite_note-130">[129]</a></sup> Domestically, much of India depended on Bengali products such as rice, silks and cotton textiles. Overseas, Europeans depended on Bengali products such as cotton textiles, silks, and opium; Bengal accounted for 40% of Dutch imports from Asia, for example, including more than 50% of textiles and around 80% of silks.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Prakash_119-2"><a href="#cite_note-Prakash-119">[118]</a></sup> From Bengal, saltpeter was also shipped to Europe, opium was sold in Indonesia, raw silk was exported to Japan and the Netherlands, and cotton and silk textiles were exported to Europe, Indonesia and Japan.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Richards1995_11-9"><a href="#cite_note-Richards1995-11">[10]</a></sup>
Akbar played a key role in establishing Bengal as a leading economic centre, as he began transforming many of the jungles there into farms. As soon as he conquered the region, he brought tools and men to clear jungles in order to expand cultivation and brought <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Sufis" title="Sufis">Sufis</a> to open the jungles to farming.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Ludden96_113-1"><a href="#cite_note-Ludden96-113">[112]</a></sup> Bengal was later described as the <i>Paradise of Nations</i> by Mughal emperors.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-131"><a href="#cite_note-131">[130]</a></sup> The Mughals introduced <a href="/wiki/Agrarian_reform" title="Agrarian reform">agrarian reforms</a>, including the modern <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Bengali_calendar" title="Bengali calendar">Bengali calendar</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-132"><a href="#cite_note-132">[131]</a></sup> The calendar played a vital role in developing and organising harvests, tax collection and Bengali culture in general, including the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Bengali_New_Year" title="Bengali New Year">New Year</a> and <a href="/wiki/Nabanna" title="Nabanna">Autumn</a> festivals. The province was a leading producer of grains, salt, fruits, liquors and wines, precious metals and ornaments.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-books.google.com_133-0"><a href="#cite_note-books.google.com-133">[132]</a></sup><sup class="noprint Inline-Template" style="white-space:nowrap;">[<i><a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Citing_sources" title="Wikipedia:Citing sources"><span title="This citation requires a reference to the specific page or range of pages in which the material appears. (January 2019)">page needed</span></a></i>]</sup> Its <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Handloom" title="Handloom">handloom</a> industry flourished under <a href="/wiki/Royal_warrant_of_appointment" title="Royal warrant of appointment">royal warrants</a>, making the region a hub of the worldwide <a href="/wiki/Muslin_trade_in_Bengal" title="Muslin trade in Bengal">muslin trade</a>, which peaked in the 17th and 18th centuries. The provincial capital <a href="/wiki/Dhaka" title="Dhaka">Dhaka</a> became the commercial capital of the empire. The Mughals expanded cultivated land in the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Bengal_delta" title="Bengal delta">Bengal delta</a> under the leadership of <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Sufi" title="Sufi">Sufis</a>, which consolidated the foundation of <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Bengali_Muslim" title="Bengali Muslim">Bengali Muslim</a> society.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-134"><a href="#cite_note-134">[133]</a></sup><sup class="noprint Inline-Template" style="white-space:nowrap;">[<i><a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Citing_sources" title="Wikipedia:Citing sources"><span title="This citation requires a reference to the specific page or range of pages in which the material appears. (January 2019)">page needed</span></a></i>]</sup>
</p><p>After 150 years of rule by Mughal <a href="/wiki/Viceroy" title="Viceroy">viceroys</a>, Bengal gained semi-independence as a <a href="/wiki/Dominion" title="Dominion">dominion</a> under the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Nawab_of_Bengal" title="Nawab of Bengal">Nawab of Bengal</a> in 1717. The Nawabs permitted European companies to set up trading posts across the region, including firms from <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/British_East_India_Company" title="British East India Company">Britain</a>, <a href="/wiki/French_East_India_Company" title="French East India Company">France</a>, the <a href="/wiki/Dutch_Bengal" title="Dutch Bengal">Netherlands</a>, <a href="/wiki/Danish_India" title="Danish India">Denmark</a>, Portugal and <a href="/wiki/Bankipur_(Bengal)" title="Bankipur (Bengal)">Austria</a>. An <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Armenian_community_of_Dhaka" title="Armenian community of Dhaka">Armenian</a> community dominated banking and shipping in major cities and towns. The Europeans regarded Bengal as the richest place for trade.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-books.google.com_133-1"><a href="#cite_note-books.google.com-133">[132]</a></sup> By the late 18th century, the <a href="/wiki/British_Empire" title="British Empire">British</a> displaced the Mughal ruling class in Bengal.
</p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Demographics">Demographics</span></h2>
<link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1033289096" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">See also: <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Demographics_of_India#History" title="Demographics of India">Demographics of India: History</a></div>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Population">Population</span></h3>
<p>India's population growth accelerated under the Mughal Empire, with an unprecedented economic and demographic upsurge which boosted the Indian population by 60%<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-mcevedy184_135-0"><a href="#cite_note-mcevedy184-135">[134]</a></sup> to 253% in 200 years during 1500–1700.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-maddison236_136-0"><a href="#cite_note-maddison236-136">[135]</a></sup> The Indian population had a faster growth during the Mughal era than at any known point in <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Indian_history" title="Indian history">Indian history</a> prior to the Mughal era.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Maddison2003_93-2"><a href="#cite_note-Maddison2003-93">[92]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-mcevedy184_135-1"><a href="#cite_note-mcevedy184-135">[134]</a></sup> By the time of Aurangzeb's reign, there were a total of 455,698 villages in the Mughal Empire.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-habib170_137-0"><a href="#cite_note-habib170-137">[136]</a></sup>
</p><p>The following table gives population estimates for the Mughal Empire, compared to the total population of India, including the regions of modern Pakistan and <a href="/wiki/Bangladesh" title="Bangladesh">Bangladesh</a>, and compared to the <a href="/wiki/World_population" title="World population">world population</a>:

<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Urbanization">Urbanization</span></h3>
<p>According to Irfan Habib Cities and towns boomed under the Mughal Empire, which had a relatively high degree of urbanization for its time, with 15% of its population living in urban centres.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Eraly2007_139-0"><a href="#cite_note-Eraly2007-139">[138]</a></sup> This was higher than the percentage of the urban population in contemporary Europe at the time and higher than that of <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/British_India" title="British India">British India</a> in the 19th century;<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Eraly2007_139-1"><a href="#cite_note-Eraly2007-139">[138]</a></sup> the level of urbanization in Europe did not reach 15% until the 19th century.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-140"><a href="#cite_note-140">[139]</a></sup>
</p><p>Under Akbar's reign in 1600, the Mughal Empire's urban population was up to 17 million people, 15% of the empire's total population. This was larger than the entire urban population in Europe at the time, and even a century later in 1700, the urban population of England, Scotland and Wales did not exceed 13% of its total population,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-habib170_137-2"><a href="#cite_note-habib170-137">[136]</a></sup> while British India had an urban population that was under 13% of its total population in 1800 and 9% in 1881, a decline from the earlier Mughal era.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-141"><a href="#cite_note-141">[140]</a></sup> By 1700, Mughal India had an urban population of 23 million people, larger than British India's urban population of 22.3 million in 1871.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-142"><a href="#cite_note-142">[141]</a></sup>
</p><p>Those estimates were criticised by <a href="/wiki/Tim_Dyson" title="Tim Dyson">Tim Dyson</a>, who consider them exaggerations. According to Dyson urbanization of Mughal empire was less than 9%.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-143"><a href="#cite_note-143">[142]</a></sup>
</p><p>The historian <a href="/wiki/Nizamuddin_Ahmad" title="Nizamuddin Ahmad">Nizamuddin Ahmad</a> (1551–1621) reported that, under Akbar's reign, there were 120 large cities and 3200 townships.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Eraly2007_139-2"><a href="#cite_note-Eraly2007-139">[138]</a></sup> A number of cities in India had a population between a quarter-million and half-million people,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Eraly2007_139-3"><a href="#cite_note-Eraly2007-139">[138]</a></sup> with larger cities including <a href="/wiki/Agra" title="Agra">Agra</a> (in <a href="/wiki/Agra_Subah" title="Agra Subah">Agra Subah</a>) with up to 800,000 people, <a href="/wiki/Lahore" title="Lahore">Lahore</a> (in <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Lahore_Subah" title="Lahore Subah">Lahore Subah</a>) with up to 700,000 people,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-144"><a href="#cite_note-144">[143]</a></sup> <a href="/wiki/Dhaka" title="Dhaka">Dhaka</a> (in <a href="/wiki/Bengal_Subah" title="Bengal Subah">Bengal Subah</a>) with over 1 million people,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-145"><a href="#cite_note-145">[144]</a></sup><sup class="noprint Inline-Template" style="white-space:nowrap;">[<i><a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Citing_sources#What_information_to_include" title="Wikipedia:Citing sources"><span title="A complete citation is needed. (October 2021)">full citation needed</span></a></i>]</sup> and Delhi (in <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Delhi_Subah" title="Delhi Subah">Delhi Subah</a>) with over 600,000 people.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Moosvi2008_146-0"><a href="#cite_note-Moosvi2008-146">[145]</a></sup>
</p><p>Cities acted as markets for the sale of goods, and provided homes for a variety of merchants, traders, shopkeepers, artisans, moneylenders, weavers, craftspeople, officials, and religious figures.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Schmidt2015_92-9"><a href="#cite_note-Schmidt2015-92">[91]</a></sup> However, a number of cities were military and political centres, rather than manufacturing or commerce centres.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Chaudhuri2008_147-0"><a href="#cite_note-Chaudhuri2008-147">[146]</a></sup>
</p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Culture">Culture</span></h2>
<link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1033289096" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">See also: <a href="/wiki/Indo-Persian_culture" title="Indo-Persian culture">Indo-Persian culture</a></div>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:202px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Mushafi-ghulam-hamdani.png"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="200" data-file-width="200" decoding="async" height="200" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/3/32/Mushafi-ghulam-hamdani.png" width="200"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Mushafi-ghulam-hamdani.png" title="Enlarge"></a></div><a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Ghulam_Hamdani_Mushafi" title="Ghulam Hamdani Mushafi">Ghulam Hamdani Mushafi</a>, the poet first believed to have coined the name "<i>Urdu</i>" around 1780 AD for a language that went by a multiplicity of names before his time.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-148"><a href="#cite_note-148">[147]</a></sup></div></div></div>
<p>The Mughal Empire was definitive in the early-modern and modern periods of South Asian history, with its legacy in India, Pakistan, Bangladesh and Afghanistan seen in cultural contributions such as:
</p>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Mir_Taqi_Mir_1786.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="790" data-file-width="564" decoding="async" height="308" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/5/5c/Mir_Taqi_Mir_1786.jpg/220px-Mir_Taqi_Mir_1786.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/5/5c/Mir_Taqi_Mir_1786.jpg/330px-Mir_Taqi_Mir_1786.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/5/5c/Mir_Taqi_Mir_1786.jpg/440px-Mir_Taqi_Mir_1786.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Mir_Taqi_Mir_1786.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Mir Taqi Mir, an Urdu poet of the 18th century Mughal Empire</div></div></div>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Taj_mahal_(1870s).jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="597" data-file-width="800" decoding="async" height="164" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/3/30/Taj_mahal_%281870s%29.jpg/220px-Taj_mahal_%281870s%29.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/3/30/Taj_mahal_%281870s%29.jpg/330px-Taj_mahal_%281870s%29.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/3/30/Taj_mahal_%281870s%29.jpg/440px-Taj_mahal_%281870s%29.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Taj_mahal_(1870s).jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>The Taj Mahal in the 1870s</div></div></div>
<ul><li>Centralised imperial rule that consolidated the smaller polities of South Asia.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-149"><a href="#cite_note-149">[148]</a></sup></li>
<li>The amalgamation of <a href="/wiki/Persian_art" title="Persian art">Persian art</a> and literature with <a href="/wiki/Indian_art" title="Indian art">Indian art</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-r1_150-0"><a href="#cite_note-r1-150">[149]</a></sup></li></ul>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Badshahi_Mosque,_Lahore._King%27s_Mosque.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="3412" data-file-width="4900" decoding="async" height="153" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/2/28/Badshahi_Mosque%2C_Lahore._King%27s_Mosque.jpg/220px-Badshahi_Mosque%2C_Lahore._King%27s_Mosque.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/2/28/Badshahi_Mosque%2C_Lahore._King%27s_Mosque.jpg/330px-Badshahi_Mosque%2C_Lahore._King%27s_Mosque.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/2/28/Badshahi_Mosque%2C_Lahore._King%27s_Mosque.jpg/440px-Badshahi_Mosque%2C_Lahore._King%27s_Mosque.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Badshahi_Mosque,_Lahore._King%27s_Mosque.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Badshahi Mosque, <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Lahore,_Punjab" title="Lahore, Punjab">Lahore, Punjab</a>, Pakistan</div></div></div>
<ul><li>The development of <a href="/wiki/Mughlai_cuisine" title="Mughlai cuisine">Mughlai cuisine</a>, an amalgamation of South Asian, Iranian and Central Asian culinary styles.</li>
<li>The development of <a href="/wiki/Mughal_clothing" title="Mughal clothing">Mughal clothing</a>, jewelry and fashion, utilizing richly decorated fabrics such as muslin, silk, brocade and velvet.</li>
<li>The standardization of the <a href="/wiki/Hindustani_language" title="Hindustani language">Hindustani language</a>, and thus the development of <a href="/wiki/Hindi" title="Hindi">Hindi</a> and <a href="/wiki/Urdu" title="Urdu">Urdu</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-151"><a href="#cite_note-151">[150]</a></sup></li>
<li>The introduction of sophisticated Iranian-style waterworks and horticulture through <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Mughal_gardens" title="Mughal gardens">Mughal gardening</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-152"><a href="#cite_note-152">[151]</a></sup></li>
<li>The introduction of <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Turkish_bath" title="Turkish bath">Turkish baths</a> into the Indian subcontinent.</li>
<li>The evolution and refinement of <a href="/wiki/Mughal_architecture" title="Mughal architecture">Mughal</a> and <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Indian_architecture" title="Indian architecture">Indian architecture</a> and in turn, the development of later Rajput and Sikh palatial architecture. A famous Mughal landmark is the <a href="/wiki/Taj_Mahal" title="Taj Mahal">Taj Mahal</a>.</li>
<li>The development of the <a href="/wiki/Pehlwani" title="Pehlwani">Pehlwani</a> style of <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Indian_wrestling" title="Indian wrestling">Indian wrestling</a>, a combination of Indian <a href="/wiki/Malla-yuddha" title="Malla-yuddha">malla-yuddha</a> and Persian <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Varzesh-e_bastani" title="Varzesh-e bastani">varzesh-e bastani</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Alter1992a_153-0"><a href="#cite_note-Alter1992a-153">[152]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Alter1992b_154-0"><a href="#cite_note-Alter1992b-154">[153]</a></sup></li>
<li>The construction of <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Maktab_(education)" title="Maktab (education)">Maktab</a> schools, where youth were taught the <a href="/wiki/Quran" title="Quran">Quran</a> and <a href="/wiki/Sharia" title="Sharia">Islamic law</a> such as the <i><a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Fatawa-e-Alamgiri" title="Fatawa-e-Alamgiri">Fatawa-i-Alamgiri</a></i> in their indigenous languages.</li>
<li>The development of <a href="/wiki/Hindustani_classical_music" title="Hindustani classical music">Hindustani classical music</a>,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-155"><a href="#cite_note-155">[154]</a></sup> and instruments such as the <a href="/wiki/Sitar" title="Sitar">sitar</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-lata_156-0"><a href="#cite_note-lata-156">[155]</a></sup></li></ul>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Fatehput_Sikiri_Buland_Darwaza_gate_2010.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="2536" data-file-width="2938" decoding="async" height="190" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/b/b5/Fatehput_Sikiri_Buland_Darwaza_gate_2010.jpg/220px-Fatehput_Sikiri_Buland_Darwaza_gate_2010.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/b/b5/Fatehput_Sikiri_Buland_Darwaza_gate_2010.jpg/330px-Fatehput_Sikiri_Buland_Darwaza_gate_2010.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/b/b5/Fatehput_Sikiri_Buland_Darwaza_gate_2010.jpg/440px-Fatehput_Sikiri_Buland_Darwaza_gate_2010.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Fatehput_Sikiri_Buland_Darwaza_gate_2010.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div><a href="/wiki/Buland_Darwaza" title="Buland Darwaza">Buland Darwaza</a> in Fatehpur Sikiri, Agra, India</div></div></div>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Architecture">Architecture</span></h3>
<link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1033289096" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Main articles: <a href="/wiki/Indo-Islamic_architecture" title="Indo-Islamic architecture">Indo-Islamic architecture</a>, <a href="/wiki/Mughal_architecture" title="Mughal architecture">Mughal architecture</a>, and <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Mughal_gardens" title="Mughal gardens">Mughal gardens</a></div><p>
The Mughals made a major contribution to the <a href="/wiki/Indian_subcontinent" title="Indian subcontinent">Indian subcontinent</a> with the development of their unique Indo-Persian <a href="/wiki/Mughal_architecture" title="Mughal architecture">architecture</a>. Many monuments were built during the Mughal era by the Muslim emperors, especially <a href="/wiki/Shah_Jahan" title="Shah Jahan">Shah Jahan</a>, including the <a href="/wiki/Taj_Mahal" title="Taj Mahal">Taj Mahal</a>—a <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/UNESCO_World_Heritage_Site" title="UNESCO World Heritage Site">UNESCO World Heritage Site</a> considered "the jewel of Muslim art in India and one of the universally admired masterpieces of the world's heritage",<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-157"><a href="#cite_note-157">[156]</a></sup> attracting 7–8 million unique visitors a year. The palaces, tombs, gardens and forts built by the dynasty stand today in <a href="/wiki/Agra" title="Agra">Agra</a>, <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Aurangabad,_Maharashtra" title="Aurangabad, Maharashtra">Aurangabad</a>, <a href="/wiki/Delhi" title="Delhi">Delhi</a>, <a href="/wiki/Dhaka" title="Dhaka">Dhaka</a>, <a href="/wiki/Fatehpur_Sikri" title="Fatehpur Sikri">Fatehpur Sikri</a>, <a href="/wiki/Jaipur" title="Jaipur">Jaipur</a>, <a href="/wiki/Lahore" title="Lahore">Lahore</a>, <a href="/wiki/Kabul" title="Kabul">Kabul</a>, <a href="/wiki/Sheikhupura" title="Sheikhupura">Sheikhupura</a>, and many other cities of India, Pakistan, Afghanistan, and Bangladesh,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-158"><a href="#cite_note-158">[157]</a></sup> such as:</p><div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:1._%E0%A6%B2%E0%A6%BE%E0%A6%B2%E0%A6%AC%E0%A6%BE%E0%A6%97%E0%A7%87%E0%A6%B0_%E0%A6%95%E0%A7%87%E0%A6%B2%E0%A7%8D%E0%A6%B2%E0%A6%BE.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" <h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Art_and_literature">Art and literature</span></h3>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:172px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Mansur-8.png"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="3407" data-file-width="2284" decoding="async" height="254" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/d/d3/Mansur-8.png/170px-Mansur-8.png" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/d/d3/Mansur-8.png/255px-Mansur-8.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/d/d3/Mansur-8.png/340px-Mansur-8.png 2x" width="170"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Mansur-8.png" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Illustration by the 17th-century Mughal artist Ustad Mansur</div></div></div>
<div class="thumb tleft"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Basawan_-_Alexander_Visits_the_Sage_Plato.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="1798" data-file-width="1169" decoding="async" height="338" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/1/17/Basawan_-_Alexander_Visits_the_Sage_Plato.jpg/220px-Basawan_-_Alexander_Visits_the_Sage_Plato.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/1/17/Basawan_-_Alexander_Visits_the_Sage_Plato.jpg/330px-Basawan_-_Alexander_Visits_the_Sage_Plato.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/1/17/Basawan_-_Alexander_Visits_the_Sage_Plato.jpg/440px-Basawan_-_Alexander_Visits_the_Sage_Plato.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Basawan_-_Alexander_Visits_the_Sage_Plato.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>"Alexander Visits the Sage Plato in His Mountain Cave"; illustration by the 16th-century Indian artist Basawan, in a folio from a quintet of the 13th-century Indian poet Amir Khusrau Dihlavi</div></div></div>
<link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1033289096" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Main articles: <a href="/wiki/Mughal_painting" title="Mughal painting">Mughal painting</a> and <a href="/wiki/Mughal_clothing" title="Mughal clothing">Mughal clothing</a></div>
<p>The <a href="/wiki/Mughal_painting" title="Mughal painting">Mughal artistic tradition</a>, mainly expressed in painted miniatures, as well as small luxury objects, was eclectic, borrowing from Iranian, Indian, Chinese and Renaissance European stylistic and thematic elements.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-159"><a href="#cite_note-159">[158]</a></sup> Mughal emperors often took in Iranian bookbinders, illustrators, painters and calligraphers from the Safavid court due to the commonalities of their Timurid styles, and due to the Mughal affinity for Iranian art and calligraphy.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-160"><a href="#cite_note-160">[159]</a></sup> Miniatures commissioned by the Mughal emperors initially focused on large projects illustrating books with eventful historical scenes and court life, but later included more single images for albums, with portraits and animal paintings displaying a profound appreciation for the serenity and beauty of the natural world.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-161"><a href="#cite_note-161">[160]</a></sup> For example, Emperor Jahangir commissioned brilliant artists such as <a href="/wiki/Ustad_Mansur" title="Ustad Mansur">Ustad Mansur</a> to realistically portray unusual flora and fauna throughout the empire.
</p><p>The literary works Akbar and Jahangir ordered to be illustrated ranged from epics like the <i><a href="/wiki/Razmnama" title="Razmnama">Razmnama</a></i> (a Persian translation of the Hindu epic, the <i><a href="/wiki/Mahabharata" title="Mahabharata">Mahabharata</a></i>) to historical memoirs or biographies of the dynasty such as the <i><a href="/wiki/Baburnama" title="Baburnama">Baburnama</a></i> and <i><a href="/wiki/Akbarnama" title="Akbarnama">Akbarnama</a></i>, and <i><a href="/wiki/Tuzk-e-Jahangiri" title="Tuzk-e-Jahangiri">Tuzk-e-Jahangiri</a></i>. Richly-finished albums (<i><a href="/wiki/Muraqqa" title="Muraqqa">muraqqa</a></i>) decorated with calligraphy and artistic scenes were mounted onto pages with decorative borders and then bound with covers of stamped and gilded or painted and lacquered leather.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-162"><a href="#cite_note-162">[161]</a></sup> Aurangzeb (1658–1707) was never an enthusiastic patron of painting, largely for religious reasons, and took a turn away from the pomp and ceremonial of the court around 1668, after which he probably commissioned no more paintings.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-163"><a href="#cite_note-163">[162]</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Language">Language</span></h3>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:172px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:India,_Mughal,_early_17th_century_-_An_Illuminated_Folio_from_the_Royal_Manuscript_of_the_Farhang-i_Jahangiri_(_-_2013.318.a_-_Cleveland_Museum_of_Art.tif"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="8000" data-file-width="4984" decoding="async" height="273" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/e/e8/India%2C_Mughal%2C_early_17th_century_-_An_Illuminated_Folio_from_the_Royal_Manuscript_of_the_Farhang-i_Jahangiri_%28_-_2013.318.a_-_Cleveland_Museum_of_Art.tif/lossy-page1-170px-India%2C_Mughal%2C_early_17th_century_-_An_Illuminated_Folio_from_the_Royal_Manuscript_of_the_Farhang-i_Jahangiri_%28_-_2013.318.a_-_Cleveland_Museum_of_Art.tif.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/e/e8/India%2C_Mughal%2C_early_17th_century_-_An_Illuminated_Folio_from_the_Royal_Manuscript_of_the_Farhang-i_Jahangiri_%28_-_2013.318.a_-_Cleveland_Museum_of_Art.tif/lossy-page1-255px-India%2C_Mughal%2C_early_17th_century_-_An_Illuminated_Folio_from_the_Royal_Manuscript_of_the_Farhang-i_Jahangiri_%28_-_2013.318.a_-_Cleveland_Museum_of_Art.tif.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/e/e8/India%2C_Mughal%2C_early_17th_century_-_An_Illuminated_Folio_from_the_Royal_Manuscript_of_the_Farhang-i_Jahangiri_%28_-_2013.318.a_-_Cleveland_Museum_of_Art.tif/lossy-page1-340px-India%2C_Mughal%2C_early_17th_century_-_An_Illuminated_Folio_from_the_Royal_Manuscript_of_the_Farhang-i_Jahangiri_%28_-_2013.318.a_-_Cleveland_Museum_of_Art.tif.jpg 2x" width="170"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:India,_Mughal,_early_17th_century_-_An_Illuminated_Folio_from_the_Royal_Manuscript_of_the_Farhang-i_Jahangiri_(_-_2013.318.a_-_Cleveland_Museum_of_Art.tif" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Folio from <i><a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Farhang-i-Jahangiri" title="Farhang-i-Jahangiri">Farhang-i-Jahangiri</a>,</i> a Persian dictionary compiled during the Mughal era.</div></div></div>
<link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1033289096" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Main articles: <a href="/wiki/Persian_language_in_the_Indian_subcontinent" title="Persian language in the Indian subcontinent">Persian language in the Indian subcontinent</a>, <a href="/wiki/Persian_and_Urdu" title="Persian and Urdu">Persian and Urdu</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Hindustani_language" title="Hindustani language">Hindustani language</a></div>
<p>Though the Mughals were of <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Turko-Mongol" title="Turko-Mongol">Turko-Mongol</a> origin, their reign enacted the revival and height of the <a href="/wiki/Persian_language" title="Persian language">Persian language</a> in the Indian subcontinent. Accompanied by literary patronage was the institutionalisation of Persian as official and courtly language; this led to Persian reaching nearly the status of a first language for many inhabitants of Mughal India.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-164"><a href="#cite_note-164">[163]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-165"><a href="#cite_note-165">[164]</a></sup> <a href="/wiki/Muzaffar_Alam" title="Muzaffar Alam">Muzaffar Alam</a> argues that the Mughals used Persian purposefully as the vehicle of an overarching <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Indo-Persian" title="Indo-Persian">Indo-Persian</a> political culture, to unite their diverse empire.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-166"><a href="#cite_note-166">[165]</a></sup> Persian had a profound impact on the languages of South Asia; one such language, today known as <a href="/wiki/Urdu" title="Urdu">Urdu</a>, developed in the imperial capital of Delhi in the late Mughal era. It began to be used as a literary language in the Mughal court from the reign of <a href="/wiki/Shah_Alam_II" title="Shah Alam II">Shah Alam II</a>, who described it as the language of his <a href="/wiki/Dastan" title="Dastan">dastans</a>,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-167"><a href="#cite_note-167">[166]</a></sup> and replaced Persian as the language of the Muslim elite.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-168"><a href="#cite_note-168">[167]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-169"><a href="#cite_note-169">[168]</a></sup> According to Qazvini, by the time of <a href="/wiki/Shah_Jahan" title="Shah Jahan">Shah Jahan</a>, the emperor was only familiar with a few <a href="/wiki/Chagatai_language" title="Chagatai language">Turki</a> words and showed little interest in the study of the language as a child.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-170"><a href="#cite_note-170">[169]</a></sup>
</p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Military">Military</span></h2>
<link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1033289096" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Further information: <a href="/wiki/Army_of_the_Mughal_Empire" title="Army of the Mughal Empire">Army of the Mughal Empire</a>, <a href="/wiki/Mughal_weapons" title="Mughal weapons">Mughal weapons</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Mughal_artillery" title="Mughal artillery">Mughal artillery</a></div>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Gunpowder_warfare">Gunpowder warfare</span></h3>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Officer_of_the_Mughal_Army,_c.1585_(colour_litho).jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="428" data-file-width="343" decoding="async" height="275" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/0/04/Officer_of_the_Mughal_Army%2C_c.1585_%28colour_litho%29.jpg/220px-Officer_of_the_Mughal_Army%2C_c.1585_%28colour_litho%29.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/0/04/Officer_of_the_Mughal_Army%2C_c.1585_%28colour_litho%29.jpg/330px-Officer_of_the_Mughal_Army%2C_c.1585_%28colour_litho%29.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/0/04/Officer_of_the_Mughal_Army%2C_c.1585_%28colour_litho%29.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Officer_of_the_Mughal_Army,_c.1585_(colour_litho).jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Mughal <a href="/wiki/Matchlock" title="Matchlock">matchlock</a> rifle, 16th century.</div></div></div>
<link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1033289096" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">See also: <a href="/wiki/Gunpowder_empires" title="Gunpowder empires">Gunpowder empires</a> and <a href="/wiki/History_of_gunpowder#India_and_the_Mughal_Empire" title="History of gunpowder">History of gunpowder: India and the Mughal Empire</a></div><p>Mughal India was one of the three Islamic <a href="/wiki/Gunpowder_empires" title="Gunpowder empires">gunpowder empires</a>, along with the <a href="/wiki/Ottoman_Empire" title="Ottoman Empire">Ottoman Empire</a> and <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Safavid_Persia" title="Safavid Persia">Safavid Persia</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Hodgson_35-1"><a href="#cite_note-Hodgson-35">[34]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Streusand_171-0"><a href="#cite_note-Streusand-171">[170]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-172"><a href="#cite_note-172">[171]</a></sup> By the time he was invited by <a href="/wiki/Lodi_dynasty" title="Lodi dynasty">Lodi</a> governor of <a href="/wiki/Lahore" title="Lahore">Lahore</a>, <a href="/wiki/Daulat_Khan_Lodi" title="Daulat Khan Lodi">Daulat Khan</a>, to support his rebellion against Lodi <a href="/wiki/Sultan" title="Sultan">Sultan</a> <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Ibrahim_Lodi" title="Ibrahim Lodi">Ibrahim Khan</a>, <a href="/wiki/Babur" title="Babur">Babur</a> was familiar with <a href="/wiki/Gunpowder" title="Gunpowder">gunpowder</a> <a href="/wiki/Firearm" title="Firearm">firearms</a> and <a href="/wiki/Field_artillery" title="Field artillery">field artillery</a>, and a method for deploying them. Babur had employed Ottoman expert <a href="/wiki/Ustad_Ali_Quli" title="Ustad Ali Quli">Ustad Ali Quli</a>, who showed Babur the standard Ottoman formation—artillery and firearm-equipped infantry protected by wagons in the centre and the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Mounted_archer" title="Mounted archer">mounted archers</a> on both wings. Babur used this formation at the <a href="/wiki/First_Battle_of_Panipat" title="First Battle of Panipat">First Battle of Panipat</a> in 1526, where the <a href="/wiki/Afghan_(ethnonym)" title="Afghan (ethnonym)">Afghan</a> and <a href="/wiki/Rajput" title="Rajput">Rajput</a> forces loyal to the <a href="/wiki/Delhi_Sultanate" title="Delhi Sultanate">Delhi Sultanate</a>, though superior in numbers but without the gunpowder weapons, were defeated. The decisive victory of the Timurid forces is one reason opponents rarely met Mughal princes in pitched battle over the course of the empire's history.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-173"><a href="#cite_note-173">[172]</a></sup> In India, guns made of <a href="/wiki/Bronze" title="Bronze">bronze</a> were recovered from <a href="/wiki/Kozhikode" title="Kozhikode">Calicut</a> (1504) and <a href="/wiki/Diu,_India" title="Diu, India">Diu</a> (1533).<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Partington226_174-0"><a href="#cite_note-Partington226-174">[173]</a></sup>
<a href="/wiki/Fathullah_Shirazi" title="Fathullah Shirazi">Fathullah Shirazi</a> (c. 1582), a Persian polymath and mechanical engineer who worked for Akbar, developed an early multi gun shot. As opposed to the <a href="/wiki/Polybolos" title="Polybolos">polybolos</a> and <a href="/wiki/Repeating_crossbow" title="Repeating crossbow">repeating crossbows</a> used earlier in <a href="/wiki/Ancient_Greece" title="Ancient Greece">ancient Greece</a> and China, respectively, Shirazi's rapid-firing gun had multiple <a href="/wiki/Gun_barrel" title="Gun barrel">gun barrels</a> that fired <a href="/wiki/Hand_cannon" title="Hand cannon">hand cannons</a> loaded with gunpowder. It may be considered a version of a <a href="/wiki/Volley_gun" title="Volley gun">volley gun</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Fathullah_Shirazi_175-0"><a href="#cite_note-Fathullah_Shirazi-175">[174]</a></sup></p><div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:The_Padshahnama,_imperial_court_guards_and_nobles.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="832" data-file-width="1300" decoding="async" height="141" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/2/2f/The_Padshahnama%2C_imperial_court_guards_and_nobles.jpg/220px-The_Padshahnama%2C_imperial_court_guards_and_nobles.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/2/2f/The_Padshahnama%2C_imperial_court_guards_and_nobles.jpg/330px-The_Padshahnama%2C_imperial_court_guards_and_nobles.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/2/2f/The_Padshahnama%2C_imperial_court_guards_and_nobles.jpg/440px-The_Padshahnama%2C_imperial_court_guards_and_nobles.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:The_Padshahnama,_imperial_court_guards_and_nobles.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Mughal <a href="/wiki/Musketeer" title="Musketeer">musketeer</a>, 17th century.</div></div></div>
<p>By the 17th century, Indians were manufacturing a diverse variety of firearms; large guns in particular, became visible in <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Tanjore" title="Tanjore">Tanjore</a>, <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Dacca" title="Dacca">Dacca</a>, <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Bijapur,_Karnataka" title="Bijapur, Karnataka">Bijapur</a> and <a href="/wiki/Murshidabad" title="Murshidabad">Murshidabad</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-176"><a href="#cite_note-176">[175]</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Rocketry_and_explosives">Rocketry and explosives</span></h3>
<link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1033289096" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">See also: <a href="/wiki/Mysorean_rockets" title="Mysorean rockets">Mysorean rockets</a> and <a href="/wiki/Congreve_rocket" title="Congreve rocket">Congreve rocket</a></div>
<p>In the sixteenth century, <a href="/wiki/Akbar" title="Akbar">Akbar</a> was the first to initiate and use metal cylinder <a href="/wiki/Rocket" title="Rocket">rockets</a> known as <i>bans</i>, particularly against <a href="/wiki/War_elephant" title="War elephant">war elephants</a>, during the Battle of Sanbal.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-177"><a href="#cite_note-177">[176]</a></sup> In 1657, the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Mughal_Army" title="Mughal Army">Mughal Army</a> used rockets during the <a href="/wiki/Siege_of_Bidar" title="Siege of Bidar">Siege of Bidar</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Yazdani15_178-0"><a href="#cite_note-Yazdani15-178">[177]</a></sup> Prince Aurangzeb's forces discharged rockets and <a href="/wiki/Grenade" title="Grenade">grenades</a> while scaling the walls. Sidi Marjan was mortally wounded when a rocket struck his large gunpowder depot, and after twenty-seven days of hard fighting Bidar was captured by the Mughals.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Yazdani15_178-1"><a href="#cite_note-Yazdani15-178">[177]</a></sup>
</p><p>In <i>A History of Greek Fire and Gunpowder</i>, <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/James_Riddick_Partington" title="James Riddick Partington">James Riddick Partington</a> described Indian rockets and <a href="/wiki/Explosive_weapon" title="Explosive weapon">explosive</a> <a href="/wiki/Land_mine" title="Land mine">mines</a>:<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Partington226_174-1"><a href="#cite_note-Partington226-174">[173]</a></sup>
</p>
<blockquote><p>The Indian war rockets were formidable weapons before such rockets were used in Europe. They had bam-boo rods, a rocket-body lashed to the rod, and iron points. They were directed at the target and fired by lighting the fuse, but the trajectory was rather erratic. The use of mines and counter-mines with explosive charges of gunpowder is mentioned for the times of Akbar and <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Jah%C4%81ngir" title="Jahāngir">Jahāngir</a>.</p></blockquote>
<p>Later, the <a href="/wiki/Mysorean_rockets" title="Mysorean rockets">Mysorean rockets</a> were upgraded versions of Mughal rockets used during the <a href="/wiki/Siege_of_Jinji" title="Siege of Jinji">Siege of Jinji</a> by the progeny of the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Nawab_of_Arcot" title="Nawab of Arcot">Nawab of Arcot</a>. <a href="/wiki/Hyder_Ali" title="Hyder Ali">Hyder Ali</a>'s father <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Fatah_Muhammad" title="Fatah Muhammad">Fatah Muhammad</a> the constable at <a href="/wiki/Budikote" title="Budikote">Budikote</a>, commanded a corps consisting of 50 rocketmen (<i>Cushoon</i>) for the Nawab of Arcot. Hyder Ali realised the importance of rockets and introduced advanced versions of metal cylinder rockets. These rockets turned fortunes in favour of the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Sultanate_of_Mysore" title="Sultanate of Mysore">Sultanate of Mysore</a> during the <a href="/wiki/Second_Anglo-Mysore_War" title="Second Anglo-Mysore War">Second Anglo-Mysore War</a>, particularly during the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Battle_of_Pollilur" title="Battle of Pollilur">Battle of Pollilur</a>. In turn, the Mysorean rockets were the basis for the <a href="/wiki/Congreve_rocket" title="Congreve rocket">Congreve rockets</a>, which Britain deployed in the <a href="/wiki/Napoleonic_Wars" title="Napoleonic Wars">Napoleonic Wars</a> against France and the <a href="/wiki/War_of_1812" title="War of 1812">War of 1812</a> against the United States.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-179"><a href="#cite_note-179">[178]</a></sup>
</p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Science">Science</span></h2>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Astronomy">Astronomy</span></h3>
<link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1033289096" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">See also: <a href="/wiki/Astronomy_in_the_medieval_Islamic_world" title="Astronomy in the medieval Islamic world">Astronomy in the medieval Islamic world</a> and <a href="/wiki/Indian_astronomy" title="Indian astronomy">Indian astronomy</a></div>
<p>While there appears to have been little concern for <a href="/wiki/Theoretical_astronomy" title="Theoretical astronomy">theoretical astronomy</a>, Mughal <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Astronomers" title="Astronomers">astronomers</a> made advances in <a href="/wiki/Observational_astronomy" title="Observational astronomy">observational astronomy</a> and produced nearly a hundred <i><a href="/wiki/Zij" title="Zij">Zij</a></i> treatises. <a href="/wiki/Humayun" title="Humayun">Humayun</a> built a personal <a href="/wiki/Observatory" title="Observatory">observatory</a> near Delhi; Jahangir and Shah Jahan were also intending to build observatories, but were unable to do so. The <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Astronomical_instruments" title="Astronomical instruments">astronomical instruments</a> and observational techniques used at the Mughal observatories were mainly derived from <a href="/wiki/Astronomy_in_the_medieval_Islamic_world" title="Astronomy in the medieval Islamic world">Islamic astronomy</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Sharma_180-0"><a href="#cite_note-Sharma-180">[179]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Baber_181-0"><a href="#cite_note-Baber-181">[180]</a></sup> In the 17th century, the Mughal Empire saw a synthesis between Islamic and <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Hindu_astronomy" title="Hindu astronomy">Hindu astronomy</a>, where Islamic observational instruments were combined with <a href="/wiki/Indian_mathematics" title="Indian mathematics">Hindu computational</a> techniques.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Sharma_180-1"><a href="#cite_note-Sharma-180">[179]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Baber_181-1"><a href="#cite_note-Baber-181">[180]</a></sup>
</p><p>During the decline of the Mughal Empire, the Hindu king <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Jai_Singh_II_of_Amber" title="Jai Singh II of Amber">Jai Singh II of Amber</a> continued the work of Mughal <a href="/wiki/Astronomy" title="Astronomy">astronomy</a>. In the early 18th century, he built several large observatories called <a href="/wiki/Jantar_Mantar" title="Jantar Mantar">Yantra Mandirs</a>, in order to rival <a href="/wiki/Ulugh_Beg" title="Ulugh Beg">Ulugh Beg</a>'s <a href="/wiki/Samarkand" title="Samarkand">Samarkand</a> <a href="/wiki/Ulugh_Beg_Observatory" title="Ulugh Beg Observatory">observatory</a>, and in order to improve on the earlier Hindu computations in the <i><a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Siddhantas" title="Siddhantas">Siddhantas</a></i> and Islamic observations in <i><a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Zij-i-Sultani" title="Zij-i-Sultani">Zij-i-Sultani</a></i>. The instruments he used were influenced by Islamic astronomy, while the computational techniques were derived from Hindu astronomy.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Sharma_180-2"><a href="#cite_note-Sharma-180">[179]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Baber_181-2"><a href="#cite_note-Baber-181">[180]</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Chemistry">Chemistry</span></h3>
<link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1033289096" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">See also: <a href="/wiki/Alchemy_and_chemistry_in_the_medieval_Islamic_world" title="Alchemy and chemistry in the medieval Islamic world">Alchemy and chemistry in the medieval Islamic world</a></div>
<p><a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Sake_Dean_Mahomed" title="Sake Dean Mahomed">Sake Dean Mahomed</a> had learned much of Mughal <a href="/wiki/Chemistry" title="Chemistry">chemistry</a> and understood the techniques used to produce various <a href="/wiki/Alkali" title="Alkali">alkali</a> and soaps to produce <a href="/wiki/Shampoo" title="Shampoo">shampoo</a>. He was also a notable writer who described the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Mughal_Emperor" title="Mughal Emperor">Mughal Emperor</a> <a href="/wiki/Shah_Alam_II" title="Shah Alam II">Shah Alam II</a> and the cities of <a href="/wiki/Allahabad" title="Allahabad">Allahabad</a> and Delhi in rich detail and also made note of the glories of the Mughal Empire.
</p><p>In Britain, Sake Dean Mahomed was appointed as <a href="/wiki/Shampoo_(massage)" title="Shampoo (massage)">shampooing surgeon</a> to both Kings <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/George_IV_of_the_United_Kingdom" title="George IV of the United Kingdom">George IV</a> and <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/William_IV_of_the_United_Kingdom" title="William IV of the United Kingdom">William IV</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Teltscher_182-0"><a href="#cite_note-Teltscher-182">[181]</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Metallurgy">Metallurgy</span></h3>
<link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1033289096" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">See also: <a href="/wiki/History_of_metallurgy_in_the_Indian_subcontinent" title="History of metallurgy in the Indian subcontinent">History of metallurgy in the Indian subcontinent</a></div>
<p>One of the most remarkable astronomical instruments invented in Mughal India is the <a href="/wiki/Lost-wax_casting" title="Lost-wax casting">lost-wax cast</a>, hollow, seamless, <a href="/wiki/Celestial_globe" title="Celestial globe">celestial globe</a>. It was invented in <a href="/wiki/Kashmir" title="Kashmir">Kashmir</a> by Ali Kashmiri ibn Luqman in 998 <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Anno_Hegirae" title="Anno Hegirae">AH</a> (1589–90 CE), and twenty other such <a href="/wiki/Globe" title="Globe">globes</a> were later produced in <a href="/wiki/Lahore" title="Lahore">Lahore</a> and Kashmir during the Mughal Empire. Before they were rediscovered in the 1980s, it was believed by modern <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Metallurgists" title="Metallurgists">metallurgists</a> to be technically impossible to produce hollow metal globes without any <a class="extiw" href="https://en.wiktionary.org/wiki/seam" title="wikt:seam">seams</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-183"><a href="#cite_note-183">[182]</a></sup>
A 17th century celestial globe was also made by Diya’ ad-din Muhammad in <a href="/wiki/Lahore" title="Lahore">Lahore</a>, 1668 (now in Pakistan).<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-184"><a href="#cite_note-184">[183]</a></sup> It is now housed at the <a href="/wiki/National_Museum_of_Scotland" title="National Museum of Scotland">National Museum of Scotland</a>.
</p>

<td style="text-align:center;"><a href="/wiki/Humayun" title="Humayun">Nasir-ud-din Muhammad Humayun</a><br/><span class="Nastaliq" dir="rtl" style="font-family: 'Jameel Noori Nastaleeq', 'Urdu Typesetting', 'Noto Nastaliq Urdu', 'Noto Nastaliq Urdu Draft', 'Hussaini Nastaleeq', 'AlQalam Taj Nastaleeq', IranNastaliq, 'Awami Nastaliq', 'Awami Nastaliq Beta3', 'Awami Nastaliq Beta2', 'Awami Nastaliq Beta1', 'Nafees Nastaleeq', 'Nafees Nastaleeq v1.01', 'Pak Nastaleeq', 'PDMS_Jauhar', 'Alvi Lahori Nastaleeq'; font-size: 110%; font-style: normal;" title="Nastaliq">نصیر الدین محمد ہمایوں</span>
</td>
<td style="text-align:center;">6 March 1508
</td>
<td style="text-align:center;">26 December 1530 – 17 May 1540
<p>9 years 4 months 21 days
</p><p>22 February 1555 – 27 January 1556
</p>
</td>
<td style="text-align:center;">27 January 1556 (aged 47)
</td>
<td style="text-align:center;">Humayun was overthrown in 1540 by <a href="/wiki/Sher_Shah_Suri" title="Sher Shah Suri">Sher Shah Suri</a> of the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Suri_dynasty" title="Suri dynasty">Suri dynasty</a> but returned to the throne in 1555 after the death of <a href="/wiki/Islam_Shah_Suri" title="Islam Shah Suri">Islam Shah Suri</a> (Sher Shah Suri's son and successor).
</td></tr>

<td style="text-align:center;">3 November 1618
</td>
<td style="text-align:center;">31 July 1658 – 3 March 1707
<p>48 years 7 months 0 days
</p>
</td>
<td style="text-align:center;">3 March 1707 (aged 88)
</td>
<td style="text-align:center;">His mother was Persian <a href="/wiki/Mumtaz_Mahal" title="Mumtaz Mahal">Mumtaz Mahal</a>. He was married to <a href="/wiki/Safavid_dynasty" title="Safavid dynasty">Safavid dynasty</a> Princess <a href="/wiki/Dilras_Banu_Begum" title="Dilras Banu Begum">Dilras Banu Begum</a>. He established Islamic law throughout India. After his death, His younger Son <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Azam_Shah" title="Azam Shah">Azam Shah</a> became the King (for 3 months) .<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-188"><a href="#cite_note-188">[187]</a></sup>
</td></tr>

<td style="text-align:center;"><a href="/wiki/Farrukhsiyar" title="Farrukhsiyar">Farrukhsiyar</a> <br/><span class="Nastaliq" dir="rtl" style="font-family: 'Jameel Noori Nastaleeq', 'Urdu Typesetting', 'Noto Nastaliq Urdu', 'Noto Nastaliq Urdu Draft', 'Hussaini Nastaleeq', 'AlQalam Taj Nastaleeq', IranNastaliq, 'Awami Nastaliq', 'Awami Nastaliq Beta3', 'Awami Nastaliq Beta2', 'Awami Nastaliq Beta1', 'Nafees Nastaleeq', 'Nafees Nastaleeq v1.01', 'Pak Nastaleeq', 'PDMS_Jauhar', 'Alvi Lahori Nastaleeq'; font-size: 110%; font-style: normal;" title="Nastaliq">فرخ سیر</span>

<td style="text-align:center;">20 August 1685
</td>
<td style="text-align:center;">11 January 1713 – 28 February 1719
<p>(6 years, 48 days)
</p>
</td>
<td style="text-align:center;">29 April 1719 (aged 33)
</td>
<td style="text-align:center;">Granted a <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Firman_(decree)" title="Firman (decree)">firman</a> to the <a href="/wiki/East_India_Company" title="East India Company">East India Company</a> in 1717 granting them duty-free trading rights for <a href="/wiki/Bengal" title="Bengal">Bengal</a>, strengthening their posts on the east coast. The firman or decree helped British East India company to import goods into Bengal without paying customs duty to the government.
</td></tr>

<td style="text-align:center;"><i>Muhammad Shah</i><br/><span class="Nastaliq" dir="rtl" style="font-family: 'Jameel Noori Nastaleeq', 'Urdu Typesetting', 'Noto Nastaliq Urdu', 'Noto Nastaliq Urdu Draft', 'Hussaini Nastaleeq', 'AlQalam Taj Nastaleeq', IranNastaliq, 'Awami Nastaliq', 'Awami Nastaliq Beta3', 'Awami Nastaliq Beta2', 'Awami Nastaliq Beta1', 'Nafees Nastaleeq', 'Nafees Nastaleeq v1.01', 'Pak Nastaleeq', 'PDMS_Jauhar', 'Alvi Lahori Nastaleeq'; font-size: 110%; font-style: normal;" title="Nastaliq">محمد شاہ</span>
</td>
<td style="text-align:center;"><a href="/wiki/Muhammad_Shah" title="Muhammad Shah">Roshan Akhtar Bahadur</a> <br/><span class="Nastaliq" dir="rtl" style="font-family: 'Jameel Noori Nastaleeq', 'Urdu Typesetting', 'Noto Nastaliq Urdu', 'Noto Nastaliq Urdu Draft', 'Hussaini Nastaleeq', 'AlQalam Taj Nastaleeq', IranNastaliq, 'Awami Nastaliq', 'Awami Nastaliq Beta3', 'Awami Nastaliq Beta2', 'Awami Nastaliq Beta1', 'Nafees Nastaleeq', 'Nafees Nastaleeq v1.01', 'Pak Nastaleeq', 'PDMS_Jauhar', 'Alvi Lahori Nastaleeq'; font-size: 110%; font-style: normal;" title="Nastaliq">روشن اختر بہادر</span>
</td>
<td style="text-align:center;">17 August 1702
</td>
<td style="text-align:center;">27 September 1719 – 26 April 1748
<p>(28 years, 212 days)
</p>
</td>
<td style="text-align:center;">26 April 1748 (aged 45)
</td>
<td style="text-align:center;">Got rid of the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Syed_Brothers" title="Syed Brothers">Syed Brothers</a>. Fought a long war with the Marathas, losing <a href="/wiki/Deccan_Plateau" title="Deccan Plateau">Deccan</a> and <a href="/wiki/Malwa" title="Malwa">Malwa</a> in the process. Suffered the invasion of <a href="/wiki/Nader_Shah" title="Nader Shah">Nader Shah</a> of Persia in 1739. He was the last emperor to possess effective control over the empire.
</td></tr>

<td style="text-align:center;"><i>Bahadur Shah II</i><br/><span class="Nastaliq" dir="rtl" style="font-family: 'Jameel Noori Nastaleeq', 'Urdu Typesetting', 'Noto Nastaliq Urdu', 'Noto Nastaliq Urdu Draft', 'Hussaini Nastaleeq', 'AlQalam Taj Nastaleeq', IranNastaliq, 'Awami Nastaliq', 'Awami Nastaliq Beta3', 'Awami Nastaliq Beta2', 'Awami Nastaliq Beta1', 'Nafees Nastaleeq', 'Nafees Nastaleeq v1.01', 'Pak Nastaleeq', 'PDMS_Jauhar', 'Alvi Lahori Nastaleeq'; font-size: 110%; font-style: normal;" title="Nastaliq">بہادر شاہ دوم</span>
</td>
<td style="text-align:center;"><a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Bahadur_Shah_II" title="Bahadur Shah II">Abu Zafar Sirajuddin Muhammad Bahadur Shah Zafar</a> <br/><span class="Nastaliq" dir="rtl" style="font-family: 'Jameel Noori Nastaleeq', 'Urdu Typesetting', 'Noto Nastaliq Urdu', 'Noto Nastaliq Urdu Draft', 'Hussaini Nastaleeq', 'AlQalam Taj Nastaleeq', IranNastaliq, 'Awami Nastaliq', 'Awami Nastaliq Beta3', 'Awami Nastaliq Beta2', 'Awami Nastaliq Beta1', 'Nafees Nastaleeq', 'Nafees Nastaleeq v1.01', 'Pak Nastaleeq', 'PDMS_Jauhar', 'Alvi Lahori Nastaleeq'; font-size: 110%; font-style: normal;" title="Nastaliq">ابو ظفر سراج اُلدین محمد بہادر شاہ ظفر</span>
</td>
<td style="text-align:center;">24 October 1775
</td>
<td style="text-align:center;">28 September 1837 – 23 September 1857 (19 years, 360 days)
</td>
<td style="text-align:center;">7 November 1862 (aged 87)
</td>
<td style="text-align:center;">Last Mughal Emperor. Deposed by the British and was exiled to <a href="/wiki/Myanmar" title="Myanmar">Burma</a> after the <a href="/wiki/Indian_Rebellion_of_1857" title="Indian Rebellion of 1857">Indian Rebellion of 1857</a>.
</td></tr></tbody></table>

<li id="cite_note-7"><span class="mw-cite-backlink"><b><a href="#cite_ref-7">^</a></b></span> <span class="reference-text">The title (Mirza) descends to all the sons of the family, without exception. In the royal family it is placed after the name instead of before it, thus, Abbas Mirza and Hosfiein Mirza. Mirza is a civil title, and Khan is a military one. The title of Khan is creative, but not hereditary.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEMorier1812601_6-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEMorier1812601-6">[6]</a></sup></span>
</li>
